Refuting the Cause-of-the-Whole Objection
Al-Jurjani refutes the objection that the independent cause of the whole could be a part of it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani refutes the objection that the independent cause of the whole could be a part of it, and why the cause of a composite of possibles must be external.

Core Idea
The cause of the whole cannot be part of it — that would lead to circularity or infinite regress.

Al-Jurjani now addresses a second objection: could the independent efficient cause of the entire system of contingent things be part of that system? The objector suggests that the cause need not be entirely external. In this passage, al-Jurjani clarifies his terms and shows that any attempt to place the cause within the composite leads to absurdity — either circular self-causation or an infinite chain of causes. This is a crucial step in proving that the ultimate cause must be transcendent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to get a sense of the argument's flow. Pay attention to the technical terms.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'cause'
Notice the distinction between the independent efficient cause and the complete cause (al-‘illa al-tāmma). The objector's confusion arises from conflating these.
The complete cause includes all conditions and is what necessitates the effect.
3
Follow the argument against part-whole causality
Trace the logical steps: if the cause of the whole were part of the whole, then it would cause itself either directly or through a regress, leading to absurdity.
4
Consider the alternative scenario
Al-Jurjani presents a scenario where each part of the composite has its own partial cause, and the sum of these causes is the cause of the whole. This is admissible.
This shows the objector's argument doesn't apply universally.
5
Reflect on the unfinished conditional
The passage ends with 'if the independent cause were...' The incomplete sentence hints that if the cause were the same for all parts, the objection would hold, but that is not the case here.
This emphasizes that the independent cause of the whole must be unique and undivided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الثاني وهو المعارضة أن التخلف العلة الفاعلية المستقلة بالمعنى الذي صورناه لا عن يمتنع [؟] إنما الممتنع هو التخلف عن العلة الفاعلية المستجمعة لجميع ما يتوقف عليه التأثير أعني العلة التامة على أنا نقول كيف يتجه علينا ما ذكرتم والمراد بقولنا علة الكل يجب أن تكون علة لكل جزء منه أن علته أي علة الجزء لا تكون خارجة عن علة الكل وبذلك الذي ذكرناه من المراد يتم مقصودنا وهو أن علة المجموع المركب من الممكنات كلها لا يجوز أن تكون جزؤه إذ يلزم حينئذ أن لا تكون علة ذلك الجزء خارجة عنه فهي إما نفسه وهو محال أو ما هو داخل فيه فينقل الكلام إليه حتى ينتهي إلى ما يكون علة لنفسه وعلى تقدير التسلسل نقول كل جزء فرض علة في تلك السلسلة فإن علته أولى منه بأن تكون علة لها فيلزم ترجيح المرجوح هذا خلف ولك أن تتمسك في إبطال علية الجزء بهذا ابتداء ولا يلزم ما ذكرتم من أحد الأمرين إذ قد تكون علة كل جزء من الأجزاء جزء علة الكل بحيث يكون الكل علة الكل فعند وجود الجزء المتقدم توجد علته التامة وعند وجود الجزء المتأخر توجد علته التامة ويكون مجموع هاتين العلتين علة تامة للكل ولا محذور فيه نعم لو كان العلة المستقلة
And regarding the second [objection], which is the counterargument: that the non-occurrence (al-takhalluf) of the independent efficient cause in the sense we have depicted is not impossible; rather, what is impossible is non-occurrence from the efficient cause that encompasses all upon which the effect depends, i.e., the complete cause (al-‘illa al-tāmma). Furthermore, we say: how does what you have mentioned apply to us? And what is meant by our statement that the cause of the whole must be a cause of every part of it is that its cause, i.e., the cause of the part, is not outside the cause of the whole. By this meaning we have mentioned, our purpose is achieved: that the cause of the composite (al-majmū‘) composed entirely of possibles cannot be a part of it, because then it would not be the cause of that part outside it. So it is either itself—which is impossible—or something within it, in which case the discussion is transferred to it until we reach something that is a cause of itself. And assuming regress (al-tasalsul), we say: every part assumed to be a cause in that chain, its cause is more deserving to be a cause of it, so the preponderance of the less deserving follows; this is a contradiction (khulf). And you may initially adhere to refuting the causality of the part by this [argument]. And what you mentioned of the two matters does not follow, for the cause of each part may be part of the cause of the whole, such that the whole is the cause of the whole. For when the prior part exists, its complete cause exists, and when the posterior part exists, its complete cause exists, and the sum of these two causes is a complete cause of the whole, and there is no problem in that. Yes, if the independent cause [were ...]
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the second objection (al-mu‘āraḍa) raised against the earlier proof. The objector claims that the independent cause of the whole might not be prohibited from being part of the whole. Al-Jurjani responds by first clarifying that the impossibility only applies to non-occurrence from the complete cause, not from any independent cause. He then explains that the intended meaning of 'the cause of the whole must be a cause of every part' is that the cause of each part is not external to the cause of the whole. This leads to the conclusion that the cause of a composite of possibles cannot be a part of it, because then it would cause itself either directly or through regress, which is absurd. Alternatively, if the cause of each part is part of the cause of the whole, then the whole cause is composed of partial causes, which is admissible. The passage ends with a conditional: if the independent cause of the whole were identical to the independent cause of each part, then the objection would hold. The argument is dense and focuses on causality and composition, showing the necessity of an external cause for the totality of possibles.

Key Terms
المعارضة (al-mu‘āraḍa)
التخلف (al-takhalluf)
العلة الفاعلية المستقلة (al-‘illa al-fā‘iliyya al-mustaqilla)
العلة التامة (al-‘illa al-tāmma)
علة الكل (illat al-kull)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
الخلف (al-khulf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'independent cause' means it cannot fail to produce the effect under any circumstances.
Correction: The text clarifies that what is necessary is non-occurrence from the complete cause, not from any independent cause. The independent cause may be a part of the cause of the whole but not the whole cause.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion about the cause of a composite of possible beings?
A) It must be a part of the composite.
B) It can be either inside or outside the composite.
C) It cannot be a part of the composite.
D) It must be identical to the composite.

Key Takeaway
The cause of a composite of possible beings cannot be part of that composite; it must be external to avoid circularity or regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything in your life—your abilities, achievements, relationships—is a composite of things that could possibly not exist, where does your ultimate reliance rest? Are you ever tempted to think you are the cause of your own success, when really you depend on something external?
?
This argument shows that a part cannot cause the whole. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes treat a part of God's creation—like wealth, status, or even your own efforts—as if it were the cause of your entire well-being? How can you realign your trust to the external, necessary Cause?
?
Consider your attachments: is there any 'part' of your world that you treat as if it were the ultimate cause of your happiness or security? What would it mean for you to let go of that illusion and acknowledge that only what is completely outside the composite of possibles can truly be the cause of all?

Scope of the Regress Argument
Al-Jurjani addresses an objection about the possibility of regress in attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani limits the proof against infinite regress to contingent events and excludes divine attributes.

Core Idea
Not all regress is equal—the proof only targets regress in possible temporal events.

Al-Jurjani now handles an objection that his third alternative should have considered regress terminating in infinite attributes. He clarifies the precise scope of his proof, pointing to a later passage where he deals with regress in attributes. This distinction is crucial for understanding the argument's limits.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noting the structure: an objection introduced by 'قيل' and an answer beginning with 'وأجيب'. Then read the translation.
Pay attention to the two different types of regress mentioned.
2
Identify the two types of regress
The objection suggests a regress that ends in eternal attributes. The answer distinguishes regress in 'حوادث ممكنة غير قائمة بذاته' (possible temporal events not subsisting in God) from regress in 'حوادث قائمة به' (temporal events subsisting in God, i.e., attributes).
3
Understand why the objection misses the mark
The objection assumes the proof covers all regress, but it only deals with one kind. Al-Jurjani's answer shows that the proof is specifically formulated for contingent events, not for attributes. He promises to address the other type later.
This clarifies that the proof is not meant to address every possible regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن لم ينته فهو الثالث قيل عليه كان الأنسب أن يقول فإن لم ينته إلى قديم أو انتهى بواسطة صفات لا تتناهى وأجيب بأن المذكور في الدليل هو التسلسل في الحوادث الممكنة الغير القائمة بذاته تعالى وأما احتمال التسلسل في الحوادث القائمة به تعالى فقد أشار إلى بطلانه بقوله واعلم أن هذا الاستدلال إلخ .
His saying: 'If it does not terminate, it is the third'—it was said against it that it would have been more appropriate for him to say 'if it does not terminate in an eternal or terminates by means of infinite attributes'. And it was answered that what is mentioned in the proof is the regress in temporal events that are possible and not subsisting in His essence, Exalted. As for the possibility of regress in temporal events subsisting in Him, He indicated its falsity by His saying: 'And know that this argument...' etc.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage deals with an objection to the third alternative in the proof (infinite regress). The objector says the proof should consider the possibility that the regress terminates in an eternal through infinite attributes. The answer clarifies that the proof specifically concerns temporal events that are possible and do not subsist in God's essence. The possibility of regress in temporal events that subsist in God (such as attributes) is not covered by this argument, and the author indicated its falsity elsewhere by saying 'And know that this argument...' etc.

Key Terms
تسلسل
حوادث
قائمة بذاته
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof against infinite regress is meant to apply to any kind of regress, including regress in divine attributes.
Correction: The proof is specifically about regress in temporal events that are possible and not subsisting in God's essence. Regress in attributes is a different matter and is addressed separately.

Checkpoint
What type of regress does the proof specifically address?
A) Regress in temporal events subsisting in God.
B) Regress in temporal events that are possible and not subsisting in God.
C) Regress in divine attributes.
D) Regress in eternal causes.

Key Takeaway
The proof against infinite regress applies only to temporal events that are possible and not subsisting in God's essence, not to divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a problem that seems to have an endless chain of causes, does this lesson help you see that the chain of created things is finite? How does that change your perspective on your own difficulties?
?
Do you truly differentiate between God's actions in creation (which are temporal events) and His eternal attributes? Why is blurring this distinction dangerous for your understanding of God?
?
If you were to defend the proof against the objection, would you be able to do so with precision? What does this careful scope tell you about the responsibility of a student of knowledge?

Objection and Answer on Infinite Regress of Attributes
Objection and answer on the type of infinite regress the proof depends on.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuance in the proof's dependence on denying infinite regress and why the succession of God's attributes is not the same as the impossible infinite regress.

Core Idea
Not all infinite regresses are impossible; only simultaneous ordered ones are.

This lesson addresses an objection raised against the innovative proof (al-burhān al-badīʿ). The objector claims that the proof also requires denying the possibility of an infinite succession of God's attributes. The answer clarifies that the impossible infinite regress is only in coexistent ordered things, not in successive attributes. A further dialogue explains why the author still explicitly mentioned the nullity of absolute regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection stating that the proof depends on denying the succession of God's attributes without end. Note the objector's claim that this is a necessary condition.
The objector concedes that the proof depends on the impossibility of an eternal other than God, but challenges its dependence on the impossibility of attribute succession.
2
Understand the answer's distinction
The answer distinguishes between two types of infinite regress: the impossible one (by agreement) is only in things that are simultaneous and ordered in existence. Succession of attributes is not simultaneous, so it does not fall under that category.
This distinction is crucial for defending the proof's scope.
3
Reflect on the author's indication
Notice that the author did indicate the nullity of absolute infinite regress in another proof. The commentator explains that the explicit mention is due to the opponent's strong dispute, emphasizing that the proof's completeness depends on refuting it.
This shows the pedagogical care taken in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( أو جاز تعاقب صفاته تعالى إلخ قيل عليه توقف البرهان المذكور على امتناع قديم سواه تعالى ،مسلم وأما توقفه على بيان امتناع تعاقب صفاته التي لا تتناهى فممنوع لأنه لو تعاقب صفاته تعالى كذلك لزم التسلسل فتندرج في الأمر الثالث وأنت خبير بأن المذكور في البرهان البديع هو التسلسل المحال بالاتفاق وهو التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة المترتبة المجتمعة في الوجود فلا يندرج تعاقب الصفات فيه على أن المصنف قد أشار إلى بطلان مطلق التسلسل في الدليل الثاني أعني قوله وإن شئت قلت مع أنه ذكر ثانياً أن تمام الدليل موقوف على بيان بطلانه فغرض الشارح أن البرهان البديع يشاركه في هذا التوقف وهذا معنى صحيح يرد عليه ما ذكر فإن قلت : إذا كان بيان لزوم التخلف في البرهان البديع ولزوم قدم الحادث في قوله وإن شئت قلت : إلخ يتضمن الإشارة إلى بطلان التسلسل مطلقاً كان عدم جواز تعاقب الصفات والحوادث الغير المتناهيتين معلوماً منه فأي حاجة إلى التصريح بأن الدليل الثاني أو الأول إنما يتم بكذا قلت : لما كان نزاع الخصم في بطلان التسلسل قويا صرح بأن تمام الدليل موقوف على إبطاله وأنه قد أبطل فيما سبق اهتماما بشأنه ولا ضير في ذلك
His saying: 'or if the succession of His attributes, exalted is He, etc.' It was said in objection: The mentioned proof depends on the impossibility of an eternal other than Him, which is granted. As for its dependence on demonstrating the impossibility of the succession of His attributes that are without end, it is denied, because if His attributes succeeded in that way, an infinite regress would result, and thus it falls under the third matter. And you are aware that what is mentioned in the innovative proof (al-burhān al-badīʿ) is the infinite regress that is impossible by agreement, which is the infinite regress in existent things that are ordered and simultaneous in existence. So the succession of attributes does not fall under it. Nevertheless, the author indicated the nullity of absolute infinite regress in the second proof, I mean his statement 'and if you wish you may say.' Moreover, he mentioned secondarily that the completeness of the proof depends on demonstrating its nullity. So the commentator's purpose is that the innovative proof shares in this dependence, and this is a correct meaning which responds to what was mentioned. If you say: Since the demonstration of the necessity of failure in the innovative proof and the necessity of the eternity of the temporal event in his statement 'and if you wish you may say' includes an indication of the nullity of absolute infinite regress, then the impermissibility of the succession of attributes and infinite events is known from it, so what need is there to state explicitly that the second or first proof is only completed by such-and-such? I say: Because the opponent's dispute over the nullity of infinite regress was strong, he explicitly stated that the completeness of the proof depends on refuting it, and that he had refuted it previously with care for its status, and there is no harm in that.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer concerning the proof's dependence on the impossibility of infinite regress of attributes. The objection claims that the proof also requires demonstrating the impossibility of an infinite succession of God's attributes, but the answer denies that, because the succession of attributes would lead to an infinite regress, but that is not the type of infinite regress that is absolutely impossible by agreement—the impossible infinite regress is only in things that are simultaneous and ordered in existence. The succession of attributes is not simultaneous, so it is not included. However, the author did indicate the nullity of absolute infinite regress in another proof. The commentator then has a dialogue: if someone says that the other proof already indicates the nullity of absolute regress, so why the explicit statement? The answer is that because the opponent strongly contested the nullity of infinite regress, the author emphasized that the proof depends on refuting it and that he had already refuted it earlier, out of care for its importance.

Key Terms
البرهان البديع
التسلسل المحال
التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة المترتبة المجتمعة
تعاقب الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All infinite regresses are absolutely impossible.
Correction: Only infinite regress in things that are ordered and simultaneous in existence is impossible by agreement; successive regress is not necessarily impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does the succession of God's attributes not fall under the impossible infinite regress?
A) Because it is not a regress
B) Because it is a succession, not a simultaneous ordered existence
C) Because God's attributes are eternal
D) Because the opponent agrees to it

Key Takeaway
The innovative proof does not depend on denying all infinite regress, only the impossible type in simultaneous ordered things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a belief about God because you thought it led to an unacceptable infinite regress, without carefully distinguishing which type of regress it would be? How might that mistake have limited your understanding of divine attributes?
?
This lesson shows that not all infinite regress is impossible. Are there areas in your spiritual life where you have accepted a limiting belief due to a fear of infinite regress (e.g., the need to perpetually seek forgiveness, or the endlessness of learning about God)? How can this distinction free you from that fear?
?
The objector in the source made an error by assuming the proof depended on denying all succession. Where in your own reasoning about faith do you tend to oversimplify or ignore important distinctions? Will you commit to studying the proofs of kalam more carefully to avoid such mistakes?

Homogeneity of Bodies and Divine Power
Objection and response on homogeneity and divine power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the kalam response refutes the claim that homogeneity of bodies is necessary for God's universal power.

Core Idea
The free agent can control specificities, so homogeneity is not required.

This lesson unpacks a subtle objection to the Ash'ari view of divine power. The objector argues that for God to create any body, bodies must be composed of identical atoms; otherwise, differences would be self-caused. The response dismantles this by showing that even with homogeneity, specific properties could be barriers, but the free agent can remove them. Let's trace the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for rhythm, then the full translation. Let the author's structure become clear.
Notice the 'qīla' (it was said) and 'fa-in qulta' (if you say) markers.
2
Identify the main objection
Find the claim that homogeneity is necessary. Why does the objector think so?
The key premise: atoms are similar, so specification by accidents must come from will.
3
Spot the counter-objection
The responder raises a challenge: even if homogeneous, why couldn't a specific property block power?
This mirrors the Mu'tazilī concept of non-existents.
4
Analyze the response
See how the response dismisses the barrier: the specific property is not caused by the free agent, so He can remove it.
The response concludes that homogeneity is not required for general power.
5
Consider the qualification
The passage ends with 'unless that specificity is among the individuators.' What does this imply?
This may leave room for a different kind of barrier, but the text cuts off here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل: ولا بد أيضاً من تجانس الأجسام لتركبها من الجواهر الفردة المتماثلة الحقيقة ليكون اختصاص بعضها ببعض الأعراض بإرادة الفاعل المختار إذ مع تخالفها جاز أن يكون ذلك الاختصاص لذواتها فلا قدرة على إيجاد بعض آخر منها. فإن قلت: لو سلم التجانس فلم لا يجوز أن يكون خصوصية بعض الأجسام مانعة مثل خصوصية بعض المعدومات على قاعدة الاعتزال، قلت: تلك الخصوصية لا تكون مستندة حينئذ لا إلى القادر المختار فله أن يعدمها ويوجد خصوصية أخرى لا تمانع القدرة اللهم إلا أن يقال الخصوصية إذا كانت من المشخصات
It was said: There must also be homogeneity of bodies because they are composed of indivisible atoms similar in essence, so that the specification of some of them with certain attributes is by the will of the free agent. For if they were different, that specification could be from their essences, and then there would be no power to create some others. If you say: If homogeneity is granted, then why is it not permissible that the specificity of some bodies be a barrier, like the specificity of some non-existents on the Mu'tazilī principle? I say: That specificity then would not be attributed to the free agent, so He can annihilate it and bring into existence another specificity that does not hinder power. Unless it is said that specificity is among the individuators.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (qīla) and its answer. The objector claims that for God's power to be general over all possible bodies, bodies must be homogeneous—composed of identical indivisible atoms—so that any differences among them (like accidents) are due to the free agent's will. If bodies were essentially different, their specific natures would be self-caused, and God could not create some other type of body. The responder then raises a counter-objection: Even granting homogeneity, why couldn't a specific property of a particular body act as a barrier, similar to how Mu'tazila think some non-existents are fixed and thus excluded from power? The answer is that such a property would not be caused by the free agent, so He can remove it and create another property that doesn't hinder power. The passage ends with a qualification: unless that specific property is among the individuators (mushakhkhiṣāt), which might imply an exception, but the response is cut off by the page break.

Key Terms
تجانس
جواهر فردة
متماثلة
فاعل مختار
خصوصية
مشخصات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Homogeneity of bodies is necessary for God's power to be universal over all bodies.
Correction: The response shows that even without homogeneity, God can create any body because He controls specificities; homogeneity is not required.

Checkpoint
How does the responder answer the objection that a specific property of a body could prevent divine power from creating it?
A) By denying the existence of such properties.
B) By stating that such properties are not caused by the free agent and He can annihilate them.
C) By arguing that bodies are not composed of atoms.
D) By claiming that specificities are eternal and cannot be removed.

Key Takeaway
The free agent's power is not limited by homogeneity or specificities, as He can always remove or replace them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a pattern or trait in your life—something you’ve labeled as ‘just the way I am’—that you have subconsciously believed is beyond Allah’s power to change? How does this passage expose the error of that belief?
?
The objector assumed that for God to act, the world must be homogeneous. Where in your own thinking have you demanded that your circumstances meet certain conditions before you trust Allah’s power? Are you willing to repent from that demand today?
?
If Allah can annihilate any specificity not from Him and replace it with another, what does that mean for the excuses you make—whether about your past, your environment, or your abilities? Will you ask Him to remove the specific barrier that has kept you from obeying His command?

Refuting Astrologers on Celestial Influence
Refutes astrologers' claim that planets cause earthly events

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and transmitted arguments used by Muslim theologians to refute the astrologers' claim that stars cause earthly events.

Core Idea
The stars do not cause events; only Allah has true causal efficacy.

In this passage, the author presents the astrologers' claim that planets govern sublunary events, then refutes it with the example of twins and the principle that only Allah is the effecter. This lesson will guide you through the argument and its implications for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the astrologers' claim
Read the first part of the passage which states that astrologers and Sabians believe planets administer earthly affairs through their positions and configurations.
Notice how they cite seasonal changes and influences on births as evidence.
2
Identify the key refutation
The author argues that rotation does not imply causality, especially since twins can have opposite fortunes despite nearly identical birth times.
This shows that the supposed effect does not always follow the cause.
3
Examine the theological grounding
Rational and transmitted proofs are invoked to show that only Allah is the effecter, negating any independent causal power in the stars.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The author concludes that the astrologers' judgments are unsound, implying that their entire system is baseless.
This reinforces the Islamic doctrine of tawhid in causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الثانية: المنجمون ومنهم الصابئية قالوا: الكواكب المتحركة بحركات الأفلاك هي المدبرات أمراً في عالمنا هذا لدوران الحوادث السفلية والتغيرات الواقعة في جوف فلك القمر وجوداً وعدماً مع مواضعها أي مواضع الكواكب في البروج وأوضاعها بعضها إلى بعض وإلى السفليات وأظهرها ما نشاهده من اختلاف الفصول الأربعة وما يتجدد فيها من الحر والبرد والاعتدال بواسطة قرب الشمس من سمت الرأس وبعدها عنه وتواسطها فيما بينهما، وتأثير الطوالع في المواليد بالسعادة والنحوسة. والجواب: أن الدوران لا يفيد العلية سيما إذا تحقق التخلف كما في توءمين أحدهما في غاية السعادة والآخر في غاية الشقاوة ولا يمكن أن يحال بذلك على ما بينهما من التفاوت في وقت الولادة لأن التفاوت بقدر درجة واحدة لا يوجب تغير الأحكام عندهم باتفاق فيما بينهم، وسيما إذ قام البرهان على نقضه فإن البراهين العقلية والنقلية شاهدة بأن لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله. كيف ونقول لهم: ما أثبتموه من الأحكام لا يستتب
The second group: the astrologers, and among them the Sabians, they said: The planets moving with the motions of the spheres are the administrators of affairs in this world of ours, because of the rotation of sublunary events and the changes occurring within the sphere of the moon in existence and non-existence, with their positions—i.e., the positions of the planets in the zodiac signs—and their configurations relative to each other and to the lower things. The most evident of that is what we observe of the difference of the four seasons and what is renewed in them of heat, cold, and moderation by means of the sun's proximity to the zenith and its distance from it and its intermediacy between the two, and the influence of ascendants on births for good or bad luck. The answer: that rotation does not indicate causality, especially when non-concomitance is realized, as in the case of twins, one of whom is in extreme happiness and the other in extreme misery, and that cannot be attributed to the difference between them in the time of birth, because a difference of one degree does not change the judgments according to them by consensus among themselves, and especially since the proof has been established against it. For rational and transmitted proofs bear witness that there is no effecter in existence except God. Moreover, we say to them: what you have established of judgments is not sound.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the astrologers (al-munajjimūn) and the Sabians, who claim that the planets, through their motions and positions, directly influence earthly events, including seasons and human fortunes. The refutation is given in several parts. First, the mere rotation of the planets does not prove causality, especially since the supposed effects are not always concomitant, as exemplified by twins who are born at almost the same time but have vastly different fortunes. Astrologers cannot explain this by a slight time difference because a one-degree difference in ascendant should not change judgments according to their own consensus. Second, rational and revealed proofs demonstrate that no effecter exists except God. Third, the judgments they claim are not reliable. The passage ends with a statement that their judgments are unsound, but the text cuts off.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Islamic refutation of astrology denies that celestial bodies have any effect on Earth, including seasons.
Correction: The refutation only denies that stars are independent causes; it does not deny that they are signs or that patterns exist, but affirms that all causation ultimately comes from Allah.

The Astrologers' Claim and the Twin Refutation
Al-Iji refutes astrologers by citing the twin phenomenon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the astrologers' claim about celestial influence and the key refutation using the example of twins.

Core Idea
Twins disprove astrology: same sky, opposite fates.

The astrologers attribute causality to planets. Al-Iji counters with a simple observation: twins born at nearly the same moment experience vastly different lives. This lesson examines that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the argument. Then read the English translation to fully understand it.
Notice the structure: first the astrologers' claim, then the refutation.
2
Identify the astrologers' claim
What exactly do the astrologers (including the Sabians) believe about the planets? Pay attention to the terms used: 'al-mudabbirat' (administrators), 'al-tawali' (ascendants).
3
Understand the twin counterexample
Why does the example of twins refute the astrologers? Consider what they believe about the time of birth and how twins differ.
The author emphasizes that even a one-degree difference does not change astrological judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الثانية : المنجمون ومنهم الصابئية قالوا : ( الكواكب ) المتحركة بحركات الأفلاك ( هي المدبرات أمراً في عالمنا هذا ( لدوران ) الحوادث السفلية والتغيرات الواقعة في جوف فلك القمر وجوداً وعدماً (مع (مواضعها أي مواضع الكواكب في البروج وأوضاعها بعضها إلى بعض وإلى ) السفليات وأظهرها ما نشاهده من اختلاف الفصول ( الأربعة وما يتجدد فيها من الحر والبرد والاعتدال بواسطة قرب الشمس من سمت الرأس وبعدها عنه وتواسطها فيما بينهما وتأثير (الطوالع في المواليد بالسعادة والنحوسة ) والجواب : أن الدوران لا يفيد العلية سيما إذا تحقق التخلف ( كما في توءمين أحدهما في غاية السعادة والآخر في غاية الشقاوة ولا يمكن أن يحال بذلك على ما بينهما من التفاوت في وقت الولادة لأن التفاوت بقدر درجة واحدة لا يوجب تغير الأحكام عندهم باتفاق فيما بينهم (و) سيما ( إذ قام البرهان على نقضه ) فإن البراهين العقلية والنقلية شاهدة بأن لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله )
The second group: the astrologers (al-munajjimun), among them the Sabians, said: The planets moving with the motions of the spheres are the administrators of affairs in this world of ours, due to the rotations of sublunary events and the changes that occur within the sphere of the moon in existence and non-existence, together with their positions—meaning the positions of the planets in the constellations (al-buruj) and their configurations relative to each other and to the lower things. The clearest of these is what we observe of the variation of the four seasons and what occurs in them of heat, cold, and moderation through the sun’s nearness to the zenith, its distance from it, and its being midway between the two, and the influence of the ascendants (al-tawali') on births for good or bad fortune. The answer: rotation does not entail causality (al-'illiyya), especially when non-concomitance is verified, as in the case of twins, one in extreme happiness and the other in extreme misery. It is not possible to attribute that to the difference between them in the time of birth, because a difference of a single degree does not cause a change in judgments according to them, unanimously among themselves. And especially since the proof has been established against it—for the rational and transmitted proofs bear witness that there is no effecter in existence except Allah.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents the astrologers’ claim and provides the initial refutation. The astrologers, including the Sabians, hold that the planets, through their motions and configurations, govern sublunary events such as seasons, heat, cold, and even the fortunes of individuals based on ascendants. The refutation is twofold: first, mere rotation or motion does not prove causality, especially since we observe exceptions—the classic counterexample of twins born under nearly identical astrological conditions yet with vastly different fates. Even the astrologers themselves agree that a one-degree difference does not change judgments, so this difference cannot explain the twins’ disparity. Second, and more fundamentally, both rational and transmitted proofs establish that no agent has any real effect in existence except Allah. Thus the astrological claims are baseless.

Correction: The twin example shows that even nearly identical birth times yield different fates, so astrology fails as a causal system.

Al-Iji asserts the exclusive causality of Allah as the final refutation.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Allah alone is the true cause of all effects in existence.

Arbitrary Assignments on Simple Spheres
Shows that differentiating degrees on a simple sphere is arbitrary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why astrological assignments of qualities and statuses to degrees and constellations are purely arbitrary if the celestial spheres are simple and homogeneous.

Core Idea
Simple spheres with equal parts forbid any intrinsic differentiation.

The author begins by highlighting a fundamental contradiction in astrological theory. Astrologers claim the spheres are simple, yet they assign diverse properties to different points on those spheres. This passage exposes that such assignments are nothing but arbitrary judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the key claim: spheres are simple with equal parts.
The author uses 'fala' (then is impossible) to show contradiction.
2
Identify the examples of arbitrary assignments
Find the list: hot/cold, bright/dark, diurnal/nocturnal, houses, exaltations, etc. Understand each is an example of assigning different properties to equal parts.
3
Understand why they are arbitrary
If parts are identical in essence (equal in mahiyya), there is no inherent basis for differentiation. Any distinction is merely a subjective imposition (pure arbitrary judgment).
The term 'tahakkum mahd' (pure arbitrary judgment) is key.
4
Reflect on the wider implication
This argument shows that the entire edifice of astrological judgments rests on arbitrary assignments, not on objective reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيف ونقول لهم : ( ما أثبتموه من الأحكام لا يستتب لكم على قواعدكم لأنكم ) قد ادعيتم أن الأفلاك بسيطة فأجزاؤها متساوية) في الماهية ( فلا يمكن حينئذ ( جعل درجة حارة أو نيرة أو نهارية و جعل درجة ( أخرى باردة أو مظلمة أو ليلية إلا تحكماً بحتاً ) ، وكذا الحال في جعل بعض البروج بيتاً لكواكب وبعضها بيتاً لكواكب أخر وفي جعل بعض الدرج شرفاً وبعضها وبالاً إلى غير ذلك من الأمور التي تدعونها فإنها كلها على تقدير البساطة تحكمات محضة . (ثم نردد ونقول : إن الفلك) إن كان بسيطاً فقد بطلت الأحكام التي تزعمونها لما ذكرناه وإلا بطل علم الهيئة إذ مبناه أن الفلك بسيط فحركاته بسيطة متشابهة في أنفسها ) فالحركات المختلفة ) والمشاهدة والمرصودة منها تقتضي محركات مختلفة ) على أوضاع متفاوتة تكون حركة كل منها وحدها متشابهة غير مختلفة، ويلزم منها حركات متخالفة ( كما عرفت وإذا بطلت ) الهيئة بطلت ( الأحكام ) النجومية ( لأنها مبنية عليها على الهيئات المتخيلة لهم وإلا فلا أوج ولا حضيض ولا وقوف ولا رجوع فكيف يثبت لها أحكام؟) مترتبة عليها. )
How? And we say to them: What you have established of judgments is not tenable on your principles, because you have claimed that the spheres are simple, so their parts are equal in essence. Then it is impossible to make one degree hot or bright or diurnal, and another degree cold or dark or nocturnal, except as pure arbitrary judgment. Likewise in making some constellations a house for planets and others a house for other planets, and in making some degrees exaltations and others dejections, and other such matters that you claim—all of them, on the assumption of simplicity, are mere arbitrary judgments. Then we oscillate and say: If the sphere is simple, then the judgments you claim are void for what we have mentioned; otherwise, the science of astronomy (al-hay'a) is void, for its basis is that the sphere is simple and its motions are simple and similar in themselves. The observed and recorded different motions require different movers in varying positions, each motion alone being similar and not varied, and from them result varied motions, as you know. And if astronomy is void, then the astrological judgments are void, because they are built upon it, based on the imagined configurations for them. Otherwise, there is no apogee, no perigee, no station, no retrogradation—so how can judgments be established that depend on them?
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents a powerful internal critique of astrological theory. The author first points out a contradiction: the astrologers assume the celestial spheres are simple, with all parts identical in essence. However, they then assign different properties to different degrees (hot/cold, bright/dark, etc.) and different cosmic statuses (houses, exaltations, dejections). Such assignments are purely arbitrary if the spheres are simple. Next, the author poses a dilemma: if the sphere is simple, then the astrological judgments (which depend on diverse positions) are invalid; but if the sphere is not simple, then the whole science of astronomy (al-hay'a) collapses, because it is founded on the premise of simplicity, uniform motion, and uniform movers. Since astronomy is the basis for astrological calculations—defining apogees, perigees, stations, and retrograde motions—its invalidation destroys astrology. Thus astrological judgments are either arbitrary or baseless.

Key Terms
الأفلاك بسيطة
تحكم محض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Astrologers assign qualities based on actual differences in the spheres.
Correction: The author shows that if spheres are simple, there are no intrinsic differences; assignments are arbitrary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes assigning hot/cold to degrees on a simple sphere impossible?
A) The spheres are too far away
B) The parts are equal in essence
C) The degrees are too small
D) The sun's heat is uniform

Key Takeaway
Astrological distinctions on simple spheres are mere arbitrary judgments.

The Dilemma That Destroys Astrology
Presents a dilemma that invalidates astrology either way.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author presents a dilemma: either the spheres are simple (making astrology void) or not simple (making astronomy void), thus astrology is invalid either way.

Core Idea
Astrology is doomed by its own premise of simple spheres.

Building on the previous argument, the author now poses a decisive dilemma. He forces the astrologer to choose: if the sphere is simple, then astrological judgments are arbitrary and void; if it is not simple, then the entire science of astronomy collapses, taking astrology with it. This passage demonstrates the internal inconsistency of astrological theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the phrase 'thumma naraddidu wa naqulu' (then we oscillate and say) introducing the dilemma.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
First horn: If sphere is simple → astrological judgments void (as argued earlier). Second horn: If sphere is not simple → science of astronomy (al-hay'a) is void.
3
Understand why astronomy is void if sphere not simple
Astronomy is built on the premise that the sphere is simple and its motions are simple. Observed varied motions would then require different movers, contradicting uniformity.
The passage says: 'its basis is that the sphere is simple and its motions are simple and similar in themselves.'
4
Connect astronomy's fall to astrology's fall
Astrological judgments depend on astronomical concepts like apogee, perigee, stations, retrograde. If astronomy is invalid, these concepts have no basis, so astrology cannot stand.
5
Conclude the dilemma's force
Either way, astrology is impossible: either directly (if simple) or indirectly via astronomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيف ونقول لهم : ( ما أثبتموه من الأحكام لا يستتب لكم على قواعدكم لأنكم ) قد ادعيتم أن الأفلاك بسيطة فأجزاؤها متساوية) في الماهية ( فلا يمكن حينئذ ( جعل درجة حارة أو نيرة أو نهارية و جعل درجة ( أخرى باردة أو مظلمة أو ليلية إلا تحكماً بحتاً ) ، وكذا الحال في جعل بعض البروج بيتاً لكواكب وبعضها بيتاً لكواكب أخر وفي جعل بعض الدرج شرفاً وبعضها وبالاً إلى غير ذلك من الأمور التي تدعونها فإنها كلها على تقدير البساطة تحكمات محضة . (ثم نردد ونقول : إن الفلك) إن كان بسيطاً فقد بطلت الأحكام التي تزعمونها لما ذكرناه وإلا بطل علم الهيئة إذ مبناه أن الفلك بسيط فحركاته بسيطة متشابهة في أنفسها ) فالحركات المختلفة ) والمشاهدة والمرصودة منها تقتضي محركات مختلفة ) على أوضاع متفاوتة تكون حركة كل منها وحدها متشابهة غير مختلفة، ويلزم منها حركات متخالفة ( كما عرفت وإذا بطلت ) الهيئة بطلت ( الأحكام ) النجومية ( لأنها مبنية عليها على الهيئات المتخيلة لهم وإلا فلا أوج ولا حضيض ولا وقوف ولا رجوع فكيف يثبت لها أحكام؟) مترتبة عليها. )
How? And we say to them: What you have established of judgments is not tenable on your principles, because you have claimed that the spheres are simple, so their parts are equal in essence. Then it is impossible to make one degree hot or bright or diurnal, and another degree cold or dark or nocturnal, except as pure arbitrary judgment. Likewise in making some constellations a house for planets and others a house for other planets, and in making some degrees exaltations and others dejections, and other such matters that you claim—all of them, on the assumption of simplicity, are mere arbitrary judgments. Then we oscillate and say: If the sphere is simple, then the judgments you claim are void for what we have mentioned; otherwise, the science of astronomy (al-hay'a) is void, for its basis is that the sphere is simple and its motions are simple and similar in themselves. The observed and recorded different motions require different movers in varying positions, each motion alone being similar and not varied, and from them result varied motions, as you know. And if astronomy is void, then the astrological judgments are void, because they are built upon it, based on the imagined configurations for them. Otherwise, there is no apogee, no perigee, no station, no retrogradation—so how can judgments be established that depend on them?
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents a powerful internal critique of astrological theory. The author first points out a contradiction: the astrologers assume the celestial spheres are simple, with all parts identical in essence. However, they then assign different properties to different degrees (hot/cold, bright/dark, etc.) and different cosmic statuses (houses, exaltations, dejections). Such assignments are purely arbitrary if the spheres are simple. Next, the author poses a dilemma: if the sphere is simple, then the astrological judgments (which depend on diverse positions) are invalid; but if the sphere is not simple, then the whole science of astronomy (al-hay'a) collapses, because it is founded on the premise of simplicity, uniform motion, and uniform movers. Since astronomy is the basis for astrological calculations—defining apogees, perigees, stations, and retrograde motions—its invalidation destroys astrology. Thus astrological judgments are either arbitrary or baseless.

Refuting the Objection on Fixed Stars and Astrology
Refutes objection that fixed stars justify astrological differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that fixed stars within constellations justify astrological differences, preserving the simplicity of the spheres.

Core Idea
Fixed stars cannot ground astrology without breaking the simplicity of the spheres.

This passage tackles a sophisticated objection: even if spheres are simple, aren't the fixed stars in constellations enough to cause astrological differences? The author systematically dismantles this claim with three arguments, each showing why fixed stars cannot bear the weight of astrological causation. Let's trace his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first two answers
Start by reading the objection from 'لا يقال' through 'وهو باطل عندكم'. Then read the author's first two responses: constellations come from the atlas sphere without stars, and fixed stars shift alignment over time.
The objection relies on fixed stars in constellations; the author counters by noting the atlas sphere is starless and that stars drift.
2
Examine the third argument: specification voids simplicity
After the objection and two answers, the author adds a third: 'ثم إنا نقول: اختصاص كل كوكب بجزء معين... يبطل بساطة الأفلاك'. Understand why this is a powerful logical move.
If each planet were assigned a specific part of the sphere, that would introduce differentiation within an assumed simple sphere.
3
Understand the gloss on proximity and alignment
The commentary clarifies that phrases like 'proximity to planets' refer to fixed stars relative to planets, not fixed stars among themselves. This avoids a misinterpretation.
The author insists that judgments rely on natures, not just relative positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : الأفلاك وإن كانت بسيطة ( متساوية الأجزاء في الماهية ( فالبروج مكوكبة ) بالثوابت المتخالفة في الطالع ( والعبرة ) في تلك الأحكام ليست بنفس البروج المتوافقة الطبيعة بل بقرب كواكبها الثابتة ) من السيارات (وبعدها) عنها ( ( ومسامتتها وعدمها فمدار الأحكام المختلفة على اختلاف أوضاع الكواكب السيارة بحركاتها من الثوابت المركوزة في البروج . ( لأنا نقول : البروج كما علم تعتبر من الفلك الأطلس الذي لا كوكب فيه على رأيهم وإن أمكن أن يقال فيه كواكب صغار غير مرئية فتختلف آثار السيارة بحلولها في البروج المختلفة الكواكب، لكن لم يقل به أحد منهم فإن قلت : البروج المعتبرة فيه وإن كانت خالية عن الكواكب إلا أنها تسامتها كواكب متخالفة الطبائع وهذا القدر كاف لاختلاف الأحكام والآثار . قلت : تلك الكواكب تزول عن المسامتة بالحركة البطيئة فيلزم أن تنتقل الأحوال من برج إلى آخر وهو باطل عندكم ) ثم ( إنا نقول : ( اختصاص كل كوكب بجزء معين من أجزاء الفلك ( يبطل بساطة (الأفلاك إذ لو كانت بسيطة لزم قوله: (من السيارات وبعدها عنها إنما قدر صلة للقرب ولم يحمل العبارة على قرب بعض الثوابت من البعض، لأن العبارة الظاهرة في ذلك هو التقارب والتسامت على أنه لا احتياج تلك الأحكام إلى اعتبار وضع بعضها مع بعض لكفاية طبعائها فيها، وأما اعتبار وضعها مع السيارات فلاختلاف آثارها باختلافه كما صرح به. قوله : ( يبطل بساطة (الأفلاك إذ لا يجوز استناد ذلك الاختصاص إلى طبائع الكواكب لأن اختلاف النسب فيما يشابه المنسوب إليه مما لا يعقل وإن كان المنسوب أمورا متخالفة الحقيقة.
It should not be said: Even if the spheres are simple, equal in parts in essence, yet the constellations are studded with fixed stars differing in the ascendant, and the consideration in those judgments is not by the constellations themselves of uniform nature, but by the proximity of their fixed stars to the planets, their distance from them, their alignment and non-alignment. So the basis of different judgments is the different positions of the planets by their motions relative to the fixed stars embedded in the constellations. For we say: The constellations, as known, are considered from the atlas sphere which has no stars in it according to their view, although it is possible to say that there are small invisible stars so that the effects of the planets vary with their entering different constellations with different stars; but no one among them has said that. If you say: The considered constellations, even if they are devoid of stars, yet they are aligned with stars of different natures, and this is sufficient for the difference of judgments and effects. I say: Those stars depart from alignment by slow motion, so it would necessitate the transfer of states from one constellation to another, and that is false according to you. Then we say: The specification of each planet to a particular part of the sphere voids the simplicity of the spheres, for if they were simple, it would necessitate... His statement: 'from the planets and their distance from them'—he only intended a connection for proximity and did not interpret the expression as the proximity of some fixed stars to others, because the apparent expression in that is approximation and alignment. And there is no need for those judgments to consider the position of some with respect to others, because their natures suffice for them; as for considering their position with respect to the planets, it is because their effects vary with their variation, as he clarified. His statement: 'it voids the simplicity of the spheres'—for it is not permissible to attribute that specification to the natures of the planets, because difference in relations to what is similar to the related is unintelligible, even if the related things are of different realities.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an objection that astrologers might raise. The objection says: Even if the spheres are simple, the constellations contain fixed stars of different natures, and the astrological judgments depend not on the constellations themselves but on the positions of the planets relative to those fixed stars. The author gives three answers: First, the constellations are considered from the atlas sphere, which is starless according to astrologers; the suggestion of invisible small stars is not made by any of them. Second, even if the constellations are aligned with stars of different natures, those stars slowly drift out of alignment over time, causing judgments to shift from one constellation to another, which astrologers reject. Third, the very fact that each planet is assigned to a specific part of the sphere contradicts the simplicity of the spheres, because specifying a particular part to a particular planet would require differentiation within a supposedly homogeneous sphere. The author concludes with a gloss explaining that differences in relation cannot be grounded in the natures of planets if the sphere is simple.

The Dilemma of Celestial Simplicity
Sanusi presents a dilemma against astrological determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key dilemma that if celestial spheres are simple, astrological distinctions become arbitrary, and if not, astronomy collapses, thereby refuting astrological claims.

Core Idea
Either the spheres are simple or not—both paths destroy astrology.

Sanusi presents a powerful dilemma aimed at astrologers who claim the heavens influence earthly events. If the celestial spheres are simple, as some assume, then any astrological differentiation is purely arbitrary. If they are not simple, then the entire science of astronomy—which relies on the simplicity of spheres—falls apart, taking astrology with it. This passage lays out that foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Read carefully the assertion that if spheres are simple, all distinctions (hot/cold, day/night, exaltation/detriment) are arbitrary. Notice the list of examples.
2
Identify the dilemma
Find where Sanusi says 'then we repeat and say' and identify the two conditional statements: if simple → astrology invalid; if not simple → astronomy invalid → astrology invalid.
3
Connect to astronomy
Understand that astrology depends on astronomical concepts like apogee/perigee. If astronomy is invalid, astrology has no basis.
4
Reflect on the force
Consider why this dilemma is considered a strong refutation—it leaves no middle ground.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثاني : في قدرته تعالى

لكم على قواعدكم لأنكم قد ادعيتم أن الأفلاك بسيطة فأجزاؤها متساوية في الماهية فلا يمكن حينئذ جعل درجة حارة أو نيرة أو نهارية و جعل درجة أخرى باردة أو مظلمة أو ليلية إلا تحكماً بحتاً، وكذا الحال في جعل بعض البروج بيتاً لكواكب وبعضها بيتاً لكواكب أخر وفي جعل بعض الدرج شرفاً وبعضها وبالاً إلى غير ذلك من الأمور التي تدعونها فإنها كلها على تقدير البساطة تحكمات محضة. ثم نردد ونقول: إن الفلك إن كان بسيطاً فقد بطلت الأحكام التي تزعمونها لما ذكرناه وإلا بطل علم الهيئة إذ مبناه أن الفلك بسيط فحركاته بسيطة متشابهة في أنفسها فالحركات المختلفة والمشاهدة والمرصودة منها تقتضي محركات مختلفة على أوضاع متفاوتة تكون حركة كل منها وحدها متشابهة غير مختلفة، ويلزم منها حركات متخالفة كما عرفت وإذا بطلت الهيئة بطلت الأحكام النجومية لأنها مبنية عليها على الهيئات المتخيلة لهم وإلا فلا أوج ولا حضيض ولا وقوف ولا رجوع فكيف يثبت لها أحكام مترتبة عليها. لا يقال: الأفلاك وإن كانت بسيطة متساوية الأجزاء في الماهية فالبروج مكوكبة بالثوابت المتخالفة في الطالع والعبرة في تلك الأحكام ليست بنفس البروج المتوافقة الطبيعة بل بقرب كواكبها الثابتة من السيارات وبعدها عنها ومسامتتها وعدمها فمدار الأحكام المختلفة على اختلاف أوضاع الكواكب السيارة بحركاتها من الثوابت المركوزة في البروج. لأنا نقول: البروج كما علم تعتبر من الفلك الأطلس الذي لا كوكب فيه على رأيهم وإن أمكن أن يقال فيه كواكب صغار غير مرئية فتختلف آثار السيارة بحلولها في البروج المختلفة الكواكب، لكن لم يقل به أحد منهم فإن قلت: البروج المعتبرة فيه وإن كانت خالية عن الكواكب إلا أنها تسامتها كواكب متخالفة الطبائع وهذا القدر كاف لاختلاف الأحكام والآثار. قلت: تلك الكواكب تزول عن المسامتة بالحركة البطيئة فيلزم أن تنتقل الأحوال من برج إلى آخر وهو باطل عندكم ثم إنا نقول: اختصاص كل كوكب بجزء معين من أجزاء الفلك يبطل بساطة الأفلاك إذ لو كانت بسيطة لزم كونه الترجيح بلا مرجج وعلى هذا فيعود الإشكال أعني بطلان الهيئة المتخيلة وما يترتب عليه من بطلان الأحكام.

قوله: (من السيارات وبعدها عنها) إنما قدر صلة للقرب ولم يحمل العبارة على قرب بعض الثوابت من البعض، لأن العبارة الظاهرة في ذلك هو التقارب والتسامت على أنه لا احتياج تلك الأحكام إلى اعتبار وضع بعضها مع بعض لكفاية طبائعها فيها، وأما اعتبار وضعها مع السيارات فلاختلاف آثارها باختلافه كما صرح به.

قوله: (يبطل بساطة الأفلاك) إذ لا يجوز استناد ذلك الاختصاص إلى طبائع الكواكب لأن اختلاف النسب فيما يشابه المنسوب إليه مما لا يعقل وإن كان المنسوب أمورا متخالفة الحقيقة.
The Fourth Observatory - The Second Objective: On His Power, Exalted.

[You say] based on your principles because you have claimed that the celestial spheres are simple, so their parts are equal in quiddity, so it is not possible then to make a degree hot or luminous or diurnal and make another degree cold or dark or nocturnal except as pure arbitrariness. Similarly for making some zodiac signs a house for planets and others a house for other planets, and making some degrees exaltations and others detriments, and other such matters you claim; all of them, on the assumption of simplicity, are pure arbitrary judgments. Then we repeat and say: If the celestial sphere is simple, then the judgments you claim are invalidated for what we have mentioned; otherwise, the science of astronomy is invalidated, since its basis is that the sphere is simple, its motions simple and similar in themselves. So the different motions, observed and recorded, necessitate different movers in varying positions, such that the motion of each alone is similar and undifferentiated, and from them result varied motions as you have known. And if astronomy is invalidated, then astrological judgments are invalidated because they are based upon it, according to the imagined configurations they have; otherwise there is no apogee, perigee, station, or retrogression, so how can judgments be established for them? It cannot be said: The spheres, even if they are simple and equal in parts in quiddity, yet the zodiac signs are studded with fixed stars differing in [astrological] ascendant, and the consideration in those judgments is not due to the signs themselves with their matching natures, but due to the proximity of their fixed stars to the planets and their distance from them, their culmination or lack thereof; so the basis of different judgments is the difference in positions of the planets by their motions relative to the fixed stars embedded in the signs. For we say: The signs, as known, are considered from the atlas sphere which has no stars according to their view, though it could be said that there are small invisible stars in it, so the effects of the planets differ by their entering the differently starred signs; but none of them has said that. If you say: The signs considered in it, even if devoid of stars, yet they are culminated by stars of different natures, and this suffices for difference of judgments and effects. I say: Those stars depart from culmination by slow motion, so it would necessitate that conditions transfer from one sign to another, which is invalid in your view. Then we say: The specification of each planet to a particular part of the celestial sphere invalidates the simplicity of the spheres; for if they were simple, it would necessitate preference without a preponderator, and thus the difficulty returns, namely the invalidation of the imagined configuration and what follows from it of invalidation of the judgments.

His saying: ('from the planets and their distance from them') — this was merely a connector for proximity, and the expression was not taken to mean proximity of some fixed stars to others, because the apparent meaning in that is proximity and culmination, and also because those judgments do not need to consider the position of some with respect to others, since their natures suffice for them. As for considering their position with respect to the planets, it is because their effects differ with the difference of that position, as he stated.

His saying: ('it invalidates the simplicity of the spheres') — because it is not permissible to ascribe that specification to the natures of the planets, since difference of relations in what resembles the thing related is unintelligible, even if the related things are of different realities.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of astrological claims based on the premise that the celestial spheres are simple (i.e., composed of identical parts in quiddity). The author begins by presenting the opponent's position: if the heavens are simple, then any astrological differentiation (hot/cold, day/night, exaltation/detriment, etc.) is purely arbitrary. The author then poses a dilemma: either the sphere is simple, in which case astrological judgments are baseless; or it is not simple, in which case the science of astronomy (which assumes simplicity) collapses, and with it all astrological judgments that depend on astronomical concepts like apogee, perigee, etc. An objection is raised: even if the sphere is simple, the zodiac signs contain fixed stars of different natures, and astrological effects depend on the positions of planets relative to these fixed stars. The author responds by noting that on their own view, the zodiac signs are considered from the atlas sphere which has no fixed stars; even if one posits invisible stars, no one has claimed that. Another objection suggests that the signs are culminated by stars of different natures even if they contain none. The author counters that those stars slowly move away from culmination, causing the supposed effects to transfer between signs—a conclusion they reject. Finally, the author argues that the specific assignment of each planet to a particular part of the sphere breaks the assumption of simplicity, because such specification would require a preponderator, leading to a vicious circle. The marginal notes clarify two points: first, that the phrase 'from the planets and their distance from them' refers to proximity and culmination, not to relative positions among fixed stars; second, that ascribing the specification to the natures of the planets is unintelligible because differences in relations do not follow from differences in the things related.

Refuting Objections: Fixed Stars and the Need for a Preponderator
Sanusi refutes objections and presents a final argument against astrological determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that fixed stars provide a basis for astrological differentiation despite the sphere's simplicity, and why the specific assignment of planets to parts of the sphere breaks simplicity.

Core Idea
Fixed stars cannot save astrology, and specifying planets to parts of the sphere requires a preponderator.

After presenting the main dilemma, Sanusi anticipates an objection: even if the spheres are simple, the fixed stars embedded in the zodiac signs differ in nature, and planets' positions relative to them could produce different effects. Sanusi counters by noting that on their own view, the zodiac signs are taken from the atlas sphere without stars, and even if stars existed, their slow motion would cause effects to shift between signs, which is unacceptable. He then delivers a final blow: the very assignment of each planet to a specific part of the sphere contradicts simplicity, as it would require an arbitrary specification without a preponderator.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage starting with 'لا يقال' which presents the astrologer's defense: fixed stars give basis for different judgments despite the sphere's simplicity.
The objection claims that the signs have fixed stars of different natures.
2
Understand the first response
Sanusi responds that the signs are considered from the atlas sphere which has no fixed stars; even if they did, slow motion would transfer effects, which astrologers reject.
The slow motion of fixed stars (precession) would cause conditions to change signs.
3
Analyze the second response
Then he argues that the specific assignment of each planet to a particular part of the sphere (e.g., a fixed degree) is itself arbitrary if the sphere is simple, requiring a preponderator.
This connects to the earlier dilemma and reinforces the invalidation.
4
Read the marginal notes
The marginal notes clarify the meaning of 'proximity and distance' and explain why specification cannot be attributed to the natures of planets.
These notes reinforce the logical flaw in the astrologer's position.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثاني : في قدرته تعالى

لكم على قواعدكم لأنكم قد ادعيتم أن الأفلاك بسيطة فأجزاؤها متساوية في الماهية فلا يمكن حينئذ جعل درجة حارة أو نيرة أو نهارية و جعل درجة أخرى باردة أو مظلمة أو ليلية إلا تحكماً بحتاً، وكذا الحال في جعل بعض البروج بيتاً لكواكب وبعضها بيتاً لكواكب أخر وفي جعل بعض الدرج شرفاً وبعضها وبالاً إلى غير ذلك من الأمور التي تدعونها فإنها كلها على تقدير البساطة تحكمات محضة. ثم نردد ونقول: إن الفلك إن كان بسيطاً فقد بطلت الأحكام التي تزعمونها لما ذكرناه وإلا بطل علم الهيئة إذ مبناه أن الفلك بسيط فحركاته بسيطة متشابهة في أنفسها فالحركات المختلفة والمشاهدة والمرصودة منها تقتضي محركات مختلفة على أوضاع متفاوتة تكون حركة كل منها وحدها متشابهة غير مختلفة، ويلزم منها حركات متخالفة كما عرفت وإذا بطلت الهيئة بطلت الأحكام النجومية لأنها مبنية عليها على الهيئات المتخيلة لهم وإلا فلا أوج ولا حضيض ولا وقوف ولا رجوع فكيف يثبت لها أحكام مترتبة عليها. لا يقال: الأفلاك وإن كانت بسيطة متساوية الأجزاء في الماهية فالبروج مكوكبة بالثوابت المتخالفة في الطالع والعبرة في تلك الأحكام ليست بنفس البروج المتوافقة الطبيعة بل بقرب كواكبها الثابتة من السيارات وبعدها عنها ومسامتتها وعدمها فمدار الأحكام المختلفة على اختلاف أوضاع الكواكب السيارة بحركاتها من الثوابت المركوزة في البروج. لأنا نقول: البروج كما علم تعتبر من الفلك الأطلس الذي لا كوكب فيه على رأيهم وإن أمكن أن يقال فيه كواكب صغار غير مرئية فتختلف آثار السيارة بحلولها في البروج المختلفة الكواكب، لكن لم يقل به أحد منهم فإن قلت: البروج المعتبرة فيه وإن كانت خالية عن الكواكب إلا أنها تسامتها كواكب متخالفة الطبائع وهذا القدر كاف لاختلاف الأحكام والآثار. قلت: تلك الكواكب تزول عن المسامتة بالحركة البطيئة فيلزم أن تنتقل الأحوال من برج إلى آخر وهو باطل عندكم ثم إنا نقول: اختصاص كل كوكب بجزء معين من أجزاء الفلك يبطل بساطة الأفلاك إذ لو كانت بسيطة لزم كونه الترجيح بلا مرجج وعلى هذا فيعود الإشكال أعني بطلان الهيئة المتخيلة وما يترتب عليه من بطلان الأحكام.

قوله: (من السيارات وبعدها عنها) إنما قدر صلة للقرب ولم يحمل العبارة على قرب بعض الثوابت من البعض، لأن العبارة الظاهرة في ذلك هو التقارب والتسامت على أنه لا احتياج تلك الأحكام إلى اعتبار وضع بعضها مع بعض لكفاية طبائعها فيها، وأما اعتبار وضعها مع السيارات فلاختلاف آثارها باختلافه كما صرح به.

قوله: (يبطل بساطة الأفلاك) إذ لا يجوز استناد ذلك الاختصاص إلى طبائع الكواكب لأن اختلاف النسب فيما يشابه المنسوب إليه مما لا يعقل وإن كان المنسوب أمورا متخالفة الحقيقة.
The Fourth Observatory - The Second Objective: On His Power, Exalted.

[You say] based on your principles because you have claimed that the celestial spheres are simple, so their parts are equal in quiddity, so it is not possible then to make a degree hot or luminous or diurnal and make another degree cold or dark or nocturnal except as pure arbitrariness. Similarly for making some zodiac signs a house for planets and others a house for other planets, and making some degrees exaltations and others detriments, and other such matters you claim; all of them, on the assumption of simplicity, are pure arbitrary judgments. Then we repeat and say: If the celestial sphere is simple, then the judgments you claim are invalidated for what we have mentioned; otherwise, the science of astronomy is invalidated, since its basis is that the sphere is simple, its motions simple and similar in themselves. So the different motions, observed and recorded, necessitate different movers in varying positions, such that the motion of each alone is similar and undifferentiated, and from them result varied motions as you have known. And if astronomy is invalidated, then astrological judgments are invalidated because they are based upon it, according to the imagined configurations they have; otherwise there is no apogee, perigee, station, or retrogression, so how can judgments be established for them? It cannot be said: The spheres, even if they are simple and equal in parts in quiddity, yet the zodiac signs are studded with fixed stars differing in [astrological] ascendant, and the consideration in those judgments is not due to the signs themselves with their matching natures, but due to the proximity of their fixed stars to the planets and their distance from them, their culmination or lack thereof; so the basis of different judgments is the difference in positions of the planets by their motions relative to the fixed stars embedded in the signs. For we say: The signs, as known, are considered from the atlas sphere which has no stars according to their view, though it could be said that there are small invisible stars in it, so the effects of the planets differ by their entering the differently starred signs; but none of them has said that. If you say: The signs considered in it, even if devoid of stars, yet they are culminated by stars of different natures, and this suffices for difference of judgments and effects. I say: Those stars depart from culmination by slow motion, so it would necessitate that conditions transfer from one sign to another, which is invalid in your view. Then we say: The specification of each planet to a particular part of the celestial sphere invalidates the simplicity of the spheres; for if they were simple, it would necessitate preference without a preponderator, and thus the difficulty returns, namely the invalidation of the imagined configuration and what follows from it of invalidation of the judgments.

His saying: ('from the planets and their distance from them') — this was merely a connector for proximity, and the expression was not taken to mean proximity of some fixed stars to others, because the apparent meaning in that is proximity and culmination, and also because those judgments do not need to consider the position of some with respect to others, since their natures suffice for them. As for considering their position with respect to the planets, it is because their effects differ with the difference of that position, as he stated.

His saying: ('it invalidates the simplicity of the spheres') — because it is not permissible to ascribe that specification to the natures of the planets, since difference of relations in what resembles the thing related is unintelligible, even if the related things are of different realities.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of astrological claims based on the premise that the celestial spheres are simple (i.e., composed of identical parts in quiddity). The author begins by presenting the opponent's position: if the heavens are simple, then any astrological differentiation (hot/cold, day/night, exaltation/detriment, etc.) is purely arbitrary. The author then poses a dilemma: either the sphere is simple, in which case astrological judgments are baseless; or it is not simple, in which case the science of astronomy (which assumes simplicity) collapses, and with it all astrological judgments that depend on astronomical concepts like apogee, perigee, etc. An objection is raised: even if the sphere is simple, the zodiac signs contain fixed stars of different natures, and astrological effects depend on the positions of planets relative to these fixed stars. The author responds by noting that on their own view, the zodiac signs are considered from the atlas sphere which has no fixed stars; even if one posits invisible stars, no one has claimed that. Another objection suggests that the signs are culminated by stars of different natures even if they contain none. The author counters that those stars slowly move away from culmination, causing the supposed effects to transfer between signs—a conclusion they reject. Finally, the author argues that the specific assignment of each planet to a particular part of the sphere breaks the assumption of simplicity, because such specification would require a preponderator, leading to a vicious circle. The marginal notes clarify two points: first, that the phrase 'from the planets and their distance from them' refers to proximity and culmination, not to relative positions among fixed stars; second, that ascribing the specification to the natures of the planets is unintelligible because differences in relations do not follow from differences in the things related.

كوكب
مسامتة
مرجّح
ثابت
سيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might believe that the presence of fixed stars in zodiac signs can justify astrological differences even if the sphere is simple.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even if stars exist, they cannot provide a stable basis due to their motion, and also that the specification of each planet to a part of the sphere itself contradicts simplicity.

Checkpoint
What is Sanusi's final argument regarding the specification of each planet to a particular celestial part?
A) It proves the planets have free will.
B) It invalidates the simplicity of the spheres.
C) It confirms the accuracy of astrology.
D) It shows the planets are made of different materials.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi systematically dismantles astrological determinism by showing that even if fixed stars exist, they introduce instability, and the very assignment of planets to specific positions requires a preponderator, violating simplicity.

?

Clarifying Necessary Consequences
The author answers an objection about the scope of necessary consequences of a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'necessary consequence' in causal knowledge is restricted to effects, and how an objection clarifies this.

Core Idea
Not every necessary consequence is an effect — only those that depend on the cause.

This lesson examines a precise objection to the claim that knowing a cause implies knowing all its necessary consequences. The objection points out that 'necessary consequence' is ambiguous: some consequences may be causes themselves or unrelated. The author responds by clarifying the intended meaning, narrowing it to effects that depend on the cause. This careful distinction prevents a misunderstanding in causal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector says that not all necessary consequences of a thing are its effects; some may be causes or something else. Understand why this is a valid concern.
Note the distinction between 'consequence' and 'effect'.
2
Understand the answer
The author redefines 'lāzim' (necessary consequence) to mean what follows and depends on the thing, i.e., an effect in a general sense. This resolves the objection.
This clarification limits the scope of the original claim.
3
Apply the distinction
Recognize that the claim about knowledge (knowing the cause implies knowing its consequences) applies only to dependent effects, not to all logical entailments.
This avoids overgeneralization in causal arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( بجميع لوازمه القريبة والبعيدة نوقش فيه بأن جميع لوازم الشيء لا يلزم أن يكون معلولات له بل قد يكون معلولاً وقد يكون علة وقد يكون غيرهما وأجيب بأن اللازم قد يطلق على ما يتبع الشيء ويحتاج إليه وهو بهذا المعني معلول لذلك الشيء في الجملة وهو المراد هاهنا .
His saying: (with all its near and far necessary consequences) was objected to by saying that not all necessary consequences of a thing are necessarily its effects; rather, it may be an effect, it may be a cause, or it may be something else. And it was answered that 'necessary consequence' may be applied to what follows the thing and needs it, and in this sense it is an effect of that thing in a general way, and that is what is intended here.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the claim that knowledge of the cause implies knowledge of all its necessary consequences (lāzim). The objection states that not every necessary consequence (lāzim) is an effect (ma‘lūl) of the thing; it could itself be a cause or something else. The response clarifies that the term 'lāzim' in this context refers specifically to what follows from the thing and depends on it (i.e., an effect). Thus, the argument's claim about knowledge of necessary consequences is limited to effects of the cause.

Key Terms
اللوازم
المعلول
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All necessary consequences of a cause are effects of that cause.
Correction: A necessary consequence may be a cause itself or unrelated; only those that depend on the cause are effects in the relevant sense.

Checkpoint
How is the term 'lāzim' (necessary consequence) understood in this passage?
A) Any attribute that is accidentally associated with the thing.
B) Anything that is logically entailed by the thing, regardless of dependency.
C) What follows the thing and depends on it, making it an effect in a general sense.
D) Only the immediate effect of the thing, not remote ones.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of a cause implies knowledge only of those necessary consequences that are its effects, not all logical entailments.

Are there blessings you expect that are not truly effects of your action but independent? How does this correction affect your sense of gratitude and trust in Allah?
