The Ash'arite Foundation: Commands, Locus, and Custom
Sanusi presents the core Ash'arite position on commands, moral evaluation, and custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'arites reconceptualize moral and religious commands based on actions as motives and custom, rather than human causal power.

Core Idea
Commands and praise depend on locus, not agency; custom suffices for obligation.

The Ash'arite response to Mu'tazila arguments begins by clarifying the basis of divine commands, praise, and blame. Sanusi explains that actions are commanding propositions because they act as motives for God to create the act. The key shift is from agent (fāʿiliyya) to locus (maḥalliyya) as the basis for moral evaluation, and from real causation to customary occurrence. This foundational perspective underlies all Ash'arite responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the foundational passage
Read the Arabic passage that sets out the Ash'arite view on commands, locus, and custom. Note the key terms: dāʿin, maḥalliyya, fāʿiliyya, ʿāda.
2
Understand the shift from agency to locus
Notice that praise and blame are based on the locus (where the act occurs) not on the agent's power. Ask why this distinction is important.
This avoids attributing independent causation to humans.
3
Reflect on custom as a sufficient basis for reward and punishment
The passage says reward and punishment are like other customary events. Consider how custom allows moral accountability without human causal power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : بعدما تقدم في الأفعال أن الأمر والنهي والتكليف بالأفعال باعتبار أنها دواع فيخلق الله الفعل عقيبها، وأن استحقاق المدح والذم باعتبار المحلية لا باعتبار الفاعلية، وترتب الثواب والعقاب كترتب سائر العاديات وأما حديث النسبة فمبني على الظاهر بحسب العرف وكلامنا في الواقع بحسب الحقيقة. قوله : ( أنه أي الجواب بعدما تقدم أنه (لم) لا يكفي إجراء العادة بخلق هذه الأفعال المتولدة بعد الفعل المباشر في ذلك ) هذا الجار متعلق بقوله : لا يكفي أي لم لا يكفي الأجزاء في جميع ما ذكر فإنه تعالى لما أجرى عادته بإيجاد هذه الأفعال التي يحكم عليها بالتوليد عقيب الفعل المباشر المقدور للعبد كفى ذلك في حسن الأمر والنهي والمدح والذم في النسبة، وإن لم تكن هذه الأفعال مقدورة لهم متولدة من أفعالهم. وأجاب الآمدي عما جعله وجها أول بما أسلفه في الأفعال المباشرة من أن كل عاقل يجد في نفسه أن فعله الاختياري مقارن لقدرته وقصده لا أن قدرته مؤثرة في فعله وكذا الحال في المتولدات قال : والذي نخصه هاهنا أنا وإن سلمنا وقوع الأفعال المباشرة بالقدرة على حسب القصد والداعية فهو غير متصور في المتولدات، إذ المتولد عندهم قد يقع بعد عجز فاعل السبب وبعد موته بدهر طويل فكيف يكون على حسب قصده وداعيته وإن سلم كونها على حسبهما لم يلزم منه أن يكون من أفعاله لأن المباشر إنما كان فعلا له لا لمجرد ذلك بل ومع استقلال قدرته بالإيجاد بلا احتياج إلى سبب، والمتولد محتاج إلى السبب قطعاً، وأجاب عما جعله وجهاً ثانياً بما سبق في خلق الأعمال الاختلاف أي التفاوت إنما هو في كثرة المقدورات لكثرة القدر وليس في ذلك ما يدل على وقوع الفعل بالقدرة وأجاب عن الوجوه الثلاثة المذكورة في الكتاب بكفاية إجراء العادة ولك أن تقول : جاز أن يكون وجود الاندفاع على حسب والإرادة بطريق الخلق على سبيل العادة وكذا الحال في تفاوت الحمل بحسب. قوله : ( إذا المتولد عندهم قد يقع بعد عجز فاعل السبب إلخ) قيل عليه : وقوع المتولد بعد عجز فاعل السبب وبعد موته لا ينافي كونه على حسب قصده وداعيته، إنما ينافيه لو كان وقوعه من الفاعل ابتداء وأما لو كان وقوعه منه باعتبار إيجاد السبب فلا نسلم المنافاة، غايته وقوع الفعل زمان عجز الفاعل أو زمان عدمه وذلك إنما يمتنع في الفعل المقدور ابتداء . قوله : ( والمتولد محتاج إلى السبب قطعاً إن أريد احتياجه إلى سبب غير فاعل المباشر وما يصدر عنه فهو ممنوع عندهم وإن أريد احتياجه إلى سبب صادر عن الفاعل فلا ينافي كونه فعلاً له فإن أفعال الباري تعالى صادرة عنه تعالى بمرجح يكون منه مع أنها أفعاله تعالى اتفاقاً.
And the answer: after what preceded regarding acts, that command, prohibition, and obligation regarding acts are considered because they are motives (dawa'i), then God creates the act after them, and that deserving of praise and blame is based on the locus (al-mahalliyya) not on agency (al-fa'iliyya), and the ordering of reward and punishment is like the ordering of other customary events. As for the talk of attribution, it is based on the apparent according to custom, while our speech is about reality according to truth. His statement: (that is, the answer, after what preceded, that (why) is it not sufficient to run custom by creating these generated acts after the direct act in that?) This particle is attached to his statement: 'is not sufficient', meaning why is it not sufficient to suffice in all that was mentioned? For when He Most High runs His custom of bringing into existence these acts that are judged to be generated after the direct act that is within the servant's power, that suffices for the goodness of command, prohibition, praise, blame, and attribution, even if these acts are not within their power nor generated from their acts. And al-Amidi answered what he made as a first aspect with what he previously stated regarding direct acts: that every rational person finds in himself that his voluntary act accompanies his power and intention, not that his power is effective in his act; similarly for generated acts. He said: what we specify here is that even if we concede the occurrence of direct acts by power according to intention and motive, it is inconceivable in generated acts, because the generated act according to them may occur after the agent of the cause becomes incapable and after his death by a long time, so how can it be according to his intention and motive? And even if we concede it is according to them, it does not necessitate that it is from his acts, because the direct act was his act not merely for that, but also with his power being independent in bringing into existence without need of a cause, while the generated act is in need of a cause absolutely. And he answered what he made as a second aspect with what preceded in the creation of acts: the variation is only in the abundance of what is within power due to abundance of power, and there is nothing in that indicating the occurrence of the act by power. And he answered the three aspects mentioned in the book with the sufficiency of running custom. And you may say: it is possible that the existence of movement according to intention and will is by way of creation as a custom, and similarly for the variation of carrying according to... His statement: (When the generated act according to them may occur after the incapacity of the agent of the cause, etc.) It was objected to him: the occurrence of a generated act after the incapacity of the agent of the cause and after his death does not negate its being according to his intention and motive; that would negate only if its occurrence were from the agent directly. But if its occurrence is from him by virtue of bringing about the cause, we do not concede the negation. At most, the act occurs at a time when the agent is incapable or non-existent, and that is impossible only for an act that is directly within power. His statement: (And the generated act is in need of a cause absolutely. If what is meant is its need for a cause other than the direct agent and what emanates from him, then it is denied by them. But if what is meant is its need for a cause emanating from the agent, then it does not negate its being his act, for the acts of God Most High emanate from Him by a preponderator from Him, yet they are His acts by agreement.)
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'arite responses to the Mu'tazilite arguments. The response begins by reiterating the Ash'arite position: commands and obligations are based on actions being motives (دواع) that prompt God to create the act, not on human power. Praise and blame are based on the locus (محلية) of the act, not on the agent (فاعلية). Reward and punishment are like other customary events. Attribution of acts in ordinary speech is based on custom, not on real causal power. Then a question is raised: why is the custom of God creating generated acts after the direct act not sufficient for obligation, praise, etc.? The answer: it is sufficient. Then al-Amidi's responses to the first and second Mu'tazilite arguments are given. To the first (the stone example), he argues that even if direct acts are accompanied by power and intention, generated acts are different because they occur after the agent is incapacitated or dead, so they cannot be based on intention. Also, direct acts are independent of a cause, while generated acts are not. To the second (variation in power), he says the variation is only in the number of acts within power, not a proof of power's causal efficacy. Then he responds to the three arguments: what is sufficient is God's custom of creating generated acts after the servant's direct act. Finally, an objection is raised and answered: the occurrence of a generated act after the agent's incapacity does not conflict with its being based on his intention if it is via his causation of the cause. And the need for a cause does not negate attribution, as God's acts are from Him via a preponderator.

Key Terms
داعية
محلية
فاعلية
عادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'arites deny all human responsibility and moral accountability.
Correction: Ash'arites ground accountability in custom and locus, not in human causal power. They maintain that commands, praise, and reward are valid based on God's custom, even if humans do not create their own acts.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'arite foundational answer, what is the basis for deserving praise and blame?
A) The agent's power and intention
B) The locus of the act
C) The generated act
D) The direct act

Key Takeaway
Ash'arites base commands, praise, and reward on custom and locus, not on human causal agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you secretly credit yourself for it? How does knowing that God creates the act after the motive reshape your understanding of your own role? Will you now thank Allah for the ability to even desire obedience?
?
If praise and blame are attached to your locus (you) but not your agency, how does that affect your view of your sins? Do you still see yourself as the sole cause, or can you take responsibility while recognizing Allah's creation? How will you change your attitude towards repentance?
?
The lesson says reward and punishment follow customary patterns, not necessary consequences. Does this make you more hopeful or more cautious about your deeds? How will this perspective influence your commitment to worship today, knowing that Allah's custom is reliable but not compelled?

Al-Amidi's Responses to the First Two Mu'tazila Arguments
Al-Amidi responds to two Mu'tazila arguments, defending Ash'arite position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's two responses: first, that generated acts are not based on intention because they occur after death/incapacity; second, variation in power does not prove causal efficacy.

Core Idea
Generated acts cannot be based on intention, and power variation only shows quantity, not causality.

Al-Amidi directly addresses Mu'tazila arguments. The first argument (stone example) claimed that generated acts are based on intention. Al-Amidi refutes by noting that generated acts often occur after the agent becomes incapable or dies, so intention cannot be present. The second argument (variation in power) claimed that differing outcomes reflect differing power. Al-Amidi counters that variation is only in the number of acts within power, not a proof of power's causal effect. He concludes that God's custom of creating generated acts after the direct act is sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Amidi's first response
Read the passage where al-Amidi responds to the first argument about the stone moving upon throwing. Note his key point: generated acts can occur after the agent's incapacity or death, so they cannot be based on present intention.
2
Analyze the second response on power variation
Read his response to the argument that varying degrees of power produce varying effects. He says variation is only in the number of possible acts, not a sign of causal influence.
3
Understand the sufficiency of custom
He then says the three arguments are answered by the sufficiency of God's custom in creating generated acts after the direct act. Reflect on how this ties back to the foundational answer.

Objection and Clarification: Generated Acts After Death and the Need for a Cause
Objection that generated acts after death negate intention-based attribution is refuted by distinguishing direct vs. indirect causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that generated acts after death negate intention-based attribution, and the response that attribution via causation of the cause is valid.

Core Idea
A generated act occurring after death does not negate its attribution to the agent if it occurs via his causation of the cause.

The final part of the passage addresses a specific objection raised against al-Amidi's first response. The objector argues that even if a generated act occurs after the agent's death, it could still be based on his prior intention if he caused the cause. The response clarifies that as long as the agent brought about the cause, the generated act can be attributed to him, even if he is now incapable. This is analogous to God's acts, which emanate from Him via a preponderator yet are still His acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the passage where an objector questions al-Amidi's claim that generated acts after death cannot be based on intention. Note the objection and the response.
2
Understand the key distinction
The response distinguishes between the act occurring directly from the agent versus via causation of the cause. If it is via the cause, the time of occurrence does not negate attribution.
3
Compare with God's acts
The passage ends by noting that God's acts are from Him via a preponderator, yet they are His acts. This analogy supports the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : بعدما تقدم في الأفعال أن الأمر والنهي والتكليف بالأفعال باعتبار أنها دواع فيخلق الله الفعل عقيبها، وأن استحقاق المدح والذم باعتبار المحلية لا باعتبار الفاعلية، وترتب الثواب والعقاب كترتب سائر العاديات وأما حديث النسبة فمبني على الظاهر بحسب العرف وكلامنا في الواقع بحسب الحقيقة. قوله : ( أنه أي الجواب بعدما تقدم أنه (لم) لا يكفي إجراء العادة بخلق هذه الأفعال المتولدة بعد الفعل المباشر في ذلك ) هذا الجار متعلق بقوله : لا يكفي أي لم لا يكفي الأجزاء في جميع ما ذكر فإنه تعالى لما أجرى عادته بإيجاد هذه الأفعال التي يحكم عليها بالتوليد عقيب الفعل المباشر المقدور للعبد كفى ذلك في حسن الأمر والنهي والمدح والذم في النسبة، وإن لم تكن هذه الأفعال مقدورة لهم متولدة من أفعالهم. وأجاب الآمدي عما جعله وجها أول بما أسلفه في الأفعال المباشرة من أن كل عاقل يجد في نفسه أن فعله الاختياري مقارن لقدرته وقصده لا أن قدرته مؤثرة في فعله وكذا الحال في المتولدات قال : والذي نخصه هاهنا أنا وإن سلمنا وقوع الأفعال المباشرة بالقدرة على حسب القصد والداعية فهو غير متصور في المتولدات، إذ المتولد عندهم قد يقع بعد عجز فاعل السبب وبعد موته بدهر طويل فكيف يكون على حسب قصده وداعيته وإن سلم كونها على حسبهما لم يلزم منه أن يكون من أفعاله لأن المباشر إنما كان فعلا له لا لمجرد ذلك بل ومع استقلال قدرته بالإيجاد بلا احتياج إلى سبب، والمتولد محتاج إلى السبب قطعاً، وأجاب عما جعله وجهاً ثانياً بما سبق في خلق الأعمال الاختلاف أي التفاوت إنما هو في كثرة المقدورات لكثرة القدر وليس في ذلك ما يدل على وقوع الفعل بالقدرة وأجاب عن الوجوه الثلاثة المذكورة في الكتاب بكفاية إجراء العادة ولك أن تقول : جاز أن يكون وجود الاندفاع على حسب والإرادة بطريق الخلق على سبيل العادة وكذا الحال في تفاوت الحمل بحسب. قوله : ( إذا المتولد عندهم قد يقع بعد عجز فاعل السبب إلخ) قيل عليه : وقوع المتولد بعد عجز فاعل السبب وبعد موته لا ينافي كونه على حسب قصده وداعيته، إنما ينافيه لو كان وقوعه من الفاعل ابتداء وأما لو كان وقوعه منه باعتبار إيجاد السبب فلا نسلم المنافاة، غايته وقوع الفعل زمان عجز الفاعل أو زمان عدمه وذلك إنما يمتنع في الفعل المقدور ابتداء . قوله : ( والمتولد محتاج إلى السبب قطعاً إن أريد احتياجه إلى سبب غير فاعل المباشر وما يصدر عنه فهو ممنوع عندهم وإن أريد احتياجه إلى سبب صادر عن الفاعل فلا ينافي كونه فعلاً له فإن أفعال الباري تعالى صادرة عنه تعالى بمرجح يكون منه مع أنها أفعاله تعالى اتفاقاً.
And the answer: after what preceded regarding acts, that command, prohibition, and obligation regarding acts are considered because they are motives (dawa'i), then God creates the act after them, and that deserving of praise and blame is based on the locus (al-mahalliyya) not on agency (al-fa'iliyya), and the ordering of reward and punishment is like the ordering of other customary events. As for the talk of attribution, it is based on the apparent according to custom, while our speech is about reality according to truth. His statement: (that is, the answer, after what preceded, that (why) is it not sufficient to run custom by creating these generated acts after the direct act in that?) This particle is attached to his statement: 'is not sufficient', meaning why is it not sufficient to suffice in all that was mentioned? For when He Most High runs His custom of bringing into existence these acts that are judged to be generated after the direct act that is within the servant's power, that suffices for the goodness of command, prohibition, praise, blame, and attribution, even if these acts are not within their power nor generated from their acts. And al-Amidi answered what he made as a first aspect with what he previously stated regarding direct acts: that every rational person finds in himself that his voluntary act accompanies his power and intention, not that his power is effective in his act; similarly for generated acts. He said: what we specify here is that even if we concede the occurrence of direct acts by power according to intention and motive, it is inconceivable in generated acts, because the generated act according to them may occur after the agent of the cause becomes incapable and after his death by a long time, so how can it be according to his intention and motive? And even if we concede it is according to them, it does not necessitate that it is from his acts, because the direct act was his act not merely for that, but also with his power being independent in bringing into existence without need of a cause, while the generated act is in need of a cause absolutely. And he answered what he made as a second aspect with what preceded in the creation of acts: the variation is only in the abundance of what is within power due to abundance of power, and there is nothing in that indicating the occurrence of the act by power. And he answered the three aspects mentioned in the book with the sufficiency of running custom. And you may say: it is possible that the existence of movement according to intention and will is by way of creation as a custom, and similarly for the variation of carrying according to... His statement: (When the generated act according to them may occur after the incapacity of the agent of the cause, etc.) It was objected to him: the occurrence of a generated act after the incapacity of the agent of the cause and after his death does not negate its being according to his intention and motive; that would negate only if its occurrence were from the agent directly. But if its occurrence is from him by virtue of bringing about the cause, we do not concede the negation. At most, the act occurs at a time when the agent is incapable or non-existent, and that is impossible only for an act that is directly within power. His statement: (And the generated act is in need of a cause absolutely. If what is meant is its need for a cause other than the direct agent and what emanates from him, then it is denied by them. But if what is meant is its need for a cause emanating from the agent, then it does not negate its being his act, for the acts of God Most High emanate from Him by a preponderator from Him, yet they are His acts by agreement.)
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'arite responses to the Mu'tazilite arguments. The response begins by reiterating the Ash'arite position: commands and obligations are based on actions being motives (دواع) that prompt God to create the act, not on human power. Praise and blame are based on the locus (محلية) of the act, not on the agent (فاعلية). Reward and punishment are like other customary events. Attribution of acts in ordinary speech is based on custom, not on real causal power. Then a question is raised: why is the custom of God creating generated acts after the direct act not sufficient for obligation, praise, etc.? The answer: it is sufficient. Then al-Amidi's responses to the first and second Mu'tazilite arguments are given. To the first (the stone example), he argues that even if direct acts are accompanied by power and intention, generated acts are different because they occur after the agent is incapacitated or dead, so they cannot be based on intention. Also, direct acts are independent of a cause, while generated acts are not. To the second (variation in power), he says the variation is only in the number of acts within power, not a proof of power's causal efficacy. Then he responds to the three arguments: what is sufficient is God's custom of creating generated acts after the servant's direct act. Finally, an objection is raised and answered: the occurrence of a generated act after the agent's incapacity does not conflict with its being based on his intention if it is via his causation of the cause. And the need for a cause does not negate attribution, as God's acts are from Him via a preponderator.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if a generated act occurs after death, it cannot be attributed to the agent at all.
Correction: The Ash'arite response shows that if the agent brought about the cause, the act is attributable to him even after death, as the act occurs via his causation of the cause.

Checkpoint
What is the key condition under which a generated act occurring after death can still be attributed to the agent?
A) The agent must be alive at the moment of the generated act
B) The generated act must be directly produced by the agent
C) The agent must have brought about the cause of the generated act
D) The agent must have intended the act at the moment of its occurrence

Key Takeaway
Attribution of a generated act to an agent is valid if the agent caused its cause, even if the act occurs after the agent's incapacity or death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a good deed you have done that may have effects beyond your death. Are you confident that this deed will be credited to you? This lesson says that if you caused the chain of causation, it will be. What cause have you set in motion today that will continue to produce good after you are gone? Are you intentional about initiating such chains, or do you only think of immediate results?
?
The passage uses God's acts as an analogy to show that indirect causation still counts as one's own act. Reflect on your own actions: Are there things you have done whose consequences you consider not your responsibility because they happened later or indirectly? How does this teaching challenge that sense of detachment? What would it mean for you to own the full ripple of your actions, even those that occur after your death?
?
This passage refines our understanding of intention: it is necessary at the time of causing the cause, not at the time of the resulting act. Does this comfort you or unsettle you? Consider a time you intended good but later saw negative outcomes. Does this teaching affect how you view your responsibility? What will you change today about how you set causes in motion?

Al-Amidi's Reply to the First Objection on Generated Acts
Al-Amidi replies to the first face of objection on generated acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Amidi argues that generated acts are not directly from the agent's power because they can occur after the agent's death, yet they can still be attributed to the agent via causation.

Core Idea
Generated acts are attributed through causation, not direct power.

In this passage, al-Amidi responds to the first objection against the theory of generated acts (tawleed). The objector claims that a generated act cannot be attributed to the agent because it occurs long after the agent's intention and even after the agent's death. Al-Amidi challenges this by distinguishing between direct acts and acts that proceed from the agent via a cause. He argues that while direct acts require independent power, generated acts only require that they originate from the agent's causality. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the subsequent clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Amidi's initial argument
Read the first part from 'وأجاب الآمدي' until 'موته بدهر طويل'. Note how he concedes the occurrence of direct acts by power but denies the same for generated acts due to the time gap.
Al-Amidi uses the example of an act occurring after the agent's death to show the impossibility of direct power.
2
Examine the second conditional
Read the part starting with 'وإن سلم كونها على حسبهما' until 'إلى سبب قطعاً'. Al-Amidi argues that even if the act accords with intention, it does not make it the agent's act because direct acts require independent power, while generated acts need a cause.
This establishes the key distinction between direct (mubashir) and generated (mutawallad) acts.
3
Analyze the marginal response
Read from 'قوله : ( إذا المتولد عندهم' to 'في الفعل المقدور ابتداء'. This marginal note clarifies that occurrence after death does not negate attribution if the act is via causing the cause.
It emphasizes that the act is attributed to the agent as the originator of the cause, not directly.
4
Understand the final analogy
Read from 'قوله : ( والمتولد محتاج إلى السبب قطعاً' to the end of the lesson's passage. Al-Amidi compares generated acts to divine acts, which proceed from God via a preponderant factor yet are still His acts.
This reinforces that needing a cause does not remove agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب الآمدي عما جعله وجها أول بما أسلفه في الأفعال المباشرة من أن كل عاقل يجد في نفسه أن فعله الاختياري مقارن لقدرته وقصده لا أن قدرته مؤثرة في فعله وكذا الحال في المتولدات قال : والذي نخصه هاهنا أنا وإن سلمنا وقوع الأفعال المباشرة بالقدرة على حسب القصد والداعية فهو غير متصور في المتولدات، إذ المتولد عندهم قد يقع بعد عجز فاعل السبب وبعد موته بدهر طويل فكيف يكون على حسب قصده وداعيته وإن سلم كونها على حسبهما لم يلزم منه أن يكون من أفعاله لأن المباشر إنما كان فعلا له لا لمجرد ذلك بل ومع استقلال قدرته بالإيجاد بلا احتياج إلى سبب، والمتولد محتاج إلى السبب قطعاً، قوله : ( إذا المتولد عندهم قد يقع بعد عجز فاعل السبب إلخ) قيل عليه : وقوع المتولد بعد عجز فاعل السبب وبعد موته لا ينافي كونه على حسب قصده ،وداعيته، إنما ينافيه لو كان وقوعه من الفاعل ،ابتداء وأما لو كان وقوعه منه باعتبار إيجاد السبب فلا نسلم المنافاة، غايته وقوع الفعل زمان عجز الفاعل أو زمان عدمه وذلك إنما يمتنع في الفعل المقدور ابتداء . قوله : ( والمتولد محتاج إلى السبب قطعاً إن أريد احتياجه إلى سبب غير فاعل المباشر وما يصدر عنه فهو ممنوع عندهم وإن أريد احتياجه إلى سبب صادر عن الفاعل فلا ينافي كونه فعلا له فإن أفعال الباري تعالى صادرة عنه تعالى بمرجح يكون منه مع أنها أفعاله تعالى اتفاقاً، وأجاب عما جعله وجهاً ثانياً بما سبق في خلق الأعمال الاختلاف أي التفاوت إنما هو في كثرة المقدورات لكثرة القدر وليس في ذلك ما يدل على وقوع الفعل بالقدرة وأجاب عن الوجوه الثلاثة المذكورة في الكتاب بكفاية إجراء العادة ولك أن تقول : جاز أن يكون وجود الاندفاع على حسب والإرادة بطريق الخلق على سبيل العادة وكذا الحال في تفاوت الحمل بحسب اختلاف القدر فلا يصح دعوى الضرورة وتأييدها.
And al-Amidi replied to what he made the first face with what he had previously presented concerning direct acts: that every rational person finds in himself that his voluntary act is concomitant with his power and intention, not that his power is effective in his act, and the same applies to generated acts. He said: What I specifically bring here is that even if we concede the occurrence of direct acts by power according to intention and motive, that is not conceivable in generated acts, since a generated act, according to them, may occur after the agent of the cause becomes incapable and after his death by a long period of time. How then could it be according to his intention and motive? And if it is conceded that it is according to them, it does not necessarily follow that it is from his acts, because the direct act was his act not merely for that reason but also because his power is independent in bringing it into existence without needing a cause, while the generated act is necessarily in need of a cause. His statement: ('If the generated act, according to them, may occur after the incapacity of the agent of the cause, etc.') It was said in reply: The occurrence of the generated act after the incapacity of the agent of the cause and after his death does not negate its being according to his intention and motive. That would only negate if its occurrence were from the agent directly. But if its occurrence were from him by virtue of bringing about the cause, we do not concede the negation. At most, the act occurs at a time when the agent is incapable or at a time of his non-existence, and that is only impossible for an act that is directly empowered. His statement: ('And the generated act is necessarily in need of a cause.') If what is meant is its need for a cause other than the direct agent and what proceeds from him, that is denied by them. If what is meant is its need for a cause proceeding from the agent, then that does not negate its being his act, for the acts of God Most High proceed from Him by a preponderant factor that comes from Him, yet they are His acts by consensus. And he replied to what he made the second face with what was previously stated in the creation of actions: variation is only in the multiplicity of what is empowered due to multiplicity of power, and in that there is no indication that the act occurs by power. And he replied to the three faces mentioned in the book by the sufficiency of the habit of action. And you may say: It is possible that the occurrence of repulsion is according to intention by way of creation as a matter of habit, and likewise the variation in carrying according to difference in power, so the claim of necessity and its confirmation are not valid.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a response to objections against the theory of generated acts (al-tawleed) in kalām. The commentator presents al-Amidi's replies to three objections (wujuh). The first objection is that generated acts cannot be attributed to the agent because they occur after his incapacity or death. Al-Amidi replies that even if we concede that direct acts are by power, generated acts are not conceivable by power since they occur long after the agent is gone. Moreover, even if they occur according to his intention, that does not make them his acts because direct acts require independent power, while generated acts need a cause. A marginal note then clarifies that occurrence after death does not negate being according to intention if it is from the agent by way of causing the cause. The second objection is about variation in capacities; the reply is that variation only indicates multiplicity of what is empowered, not that the act occurs by power. The third objection is answered by the sufficiency of habit (kifāyat ijrā' al-'ādah): God habitually creates the generated act after the direct act, so the commands, prohibitions, praise, and blame are valid based on that habit. The commentator adds that the claim of necessity (ḍarūra) is baseless.

المتولد
المباشر
القدرة
القصد
الداعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think generated acts are impossible because they occur after the agent's death and thus cannot be attributed to the agent.
Correction: Al-Amidi shows that though generated acts cannot be directly empowered, they are still attributable to the agent as the originator of the cause, just as divine acts are attributed to God despite being via a preponderant factor.

Checkpoint
According to al-Amidi, why can a generated act not be considered directly from the agent's power?
A) Because the agent's power is ineffective
B) Because it may occur after the agent's death, long after the intention
C) Because the agent did not intend it
D) Because it is created by God directly

Key Takeaway
Generated acts are attributed to the agent through causality, not through direct empowerment, and this attribution remains valid even when the act occurs after the agent's death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that some of your actions may continue to generate effects after you are gone, how does that affect what you choose to do today? What specific steps will you take to ensure that the generated acts you leave behind align with your intention to please Allah?
?
Al-Amidi argues that even if the act occurs after your death, it is still attributed to you if you set the cause. Are you conscious that every cause you put in motion—your words, your habits, your influence on others—will continue to be your responsibility? What change do you need to make in your daily choices?
?
The line between direct acts and generated acts reveals that your power is not independent; it is merely conjunct with your intention. Does this humble your sense of self-sufficiency? In what areas of your life do you need to acknowledge that you are merely a cause, not the ultimate agent?

The Sufficiency of Habit in Explaining Generated Acts
Al-Amidi replies to the second and third objections using the concept of habit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Amidi uses the concept of habit (al-ada) to refute the remaining objections and why the claim of necessity is invalid.

Core Idea
Habit, not power, accounts for variation and occurrence of generated acts.

Al-Amidi now addresses the second and third objections against generated acts. The second objection concerns variation in capacities: if we see different people carrying different loads, does that indicate the act occurs by power? The third objection is a combination of three faces from the book. Al-Amidi replies with a unified answer: the habit of God (ijra' al-ada) suffices to explain why generated acts occur following direct acts. He also adds a final note that the claim of necessity is baseless. This lesson explores these replies and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the reply to the second face
Read from 'وأجاب عما جعله وجهاً ثانياً' to 'يدل على وقوع الفعل بالقدرة'. Note that variation in what is empowered is due to multiplicity of power, not an indication that the act occurs by power.
This refutes the idea that observable variation implies direct power over the generated act.
2
Read the reply to the three faces via habit
Read from 'وأجاب عن الوجوه الثلاثة' to 'بكفاية إجراء العادة'. Al-Amidi simply says that the habit of action (al-ada) is sufficient to account for the occurrence of generated acts.
Habit here means God's consistent custom of creating the generated act after the direct act.
3
Examine the final concluding note
Read from 'ولك أن تقول' to the end. The commentator suggests that it is possible that repulsion occurs according to intention by way of creation habitually, so the claim of necessity is invalid.
This reinforces that nothing forces the conclusion that the act is directly from the agent's power.

وأجاب الآمدي عما جعله وجها أول بما أسلفه في الأفعال المباشرة من أن كل عاقل يجد في نفسه أن فعله الاختياري مقارن لقدرته وقصده لا أن قدرته مؤثرة في فعله وكذا الحال في المتولدات قال : والذي نخصه هاهنا أنا وإن سلمنا وقوع الأفعال المباشرة بالقدرة على حسب القصد والداعية فهو غير متصور في المتولدات، إذ المتولد عندهم قد يقع بعد عجز فاعل السبب وبعد موته بدهر طويل فكيف يكون على حسب قصده وداعيته وإن سلم كونها على حسبهما لم يلزم منه أن يكون من أفعاله لأن المباشر إنما كان فعلا له لا لمجرد ذلك بل ومع استقلال قدرته بالإيجاد بلا احتياج إلى سبب، والمتولد محتاج إلى السبب قطعاً، قوله : ( إذا المتولد عندهم قد يقع بعد عجز فاعل السبب إلخ) قيل عليه : وقوع المتولد بعد عجز فاعل السبب وبعد موته لا ينافي كونه على حسب قصده ،وداعيته، إنما ينافيه لو كان وقوعه من الفاعل ،ابتداء وأما لو كان وقوعه منه باعتبار إيجاد السبب فلا نسلم المنافاة، غايته وقوع الفعل زمان عجز الفاعل أو زمان عدمه وذلك إنما يمتنع في الفعل المقدور ابتداء . قوله : ( والمتولد محتاج إلى السبب قطعاً إن أريد احتياجه إلى سبب غير فاعل المباشر وما يصدر عنه فهو ممنوع عندهم وإن أريد احتياجه إلى سبب صادر عن الفاعل فلا ينافي كونه فعلا له فإن أفعال الباري تعالى صادرة عنه تعالى بمرجح يكون منه مع أنها أفعاله تعالى اتفاقاً، وأجاب عما جعله وجهاً ثانياً بما سبق في خلق الأعمال الاختلاف أي التفاوت إنما هو في كثرة المقدورات لكثرة القدر وليس في ذلك ما يدل على وقوع الفعل بالقدرة وأجاب عن الوجوه الثلاثة المذكورة في الكتاب بكفاية إجراء العادة ولك أن تقول : جاز أن يكون وجود الاندفاع على حسب والإرادة بطريق الخلق على سبيل العادة وكذا الحال في تفاوت الحمل بحسب اختلاف القدر فلا يصح دعوى الضرورة وتأييدها.
And al-Amidi replied to what he made the first face with what he had previously presented concerning direct acts: that every rational person finds in himself that his voluntary act is concomitant with his power and intention, not that his power is effective in his act, and the same applies to generated acts. He said: What I specifically bring here is that even if we concede the occurrence of direct acts by power according to intention and motive, that is not conceivable in generated acts, since a generated act, according to them, may occur after the agent of the cause becomes incapable and after his death by a long period of time. How then could it be according to his intention and motive? And if it is conceded that it is according to them, it does not necessarily follow that it is from his acts, because the direct act was his act not merely for that reason but also because his power is independent in bringing it into existence without needing a cause, while the generated act is necessarily in need of a cause. His statement: ('If the generated act, according to them, may occur after the incapacity of the agent of the cause, etc.') It was said in reply: The occurrence of the generated act after the incapacity of the agent of the cause and after his death does not negate its being according to his intention and motive. That would only negate if its occurrence were from the agent directly. But if its occurrence were from him by virtue of bringing about the cause, we do not concede the negation. At most, the act occurs at a time when the agent is incapable or at a time of his non-existence, and that is only impossible for an act that is directly empowered. His statement: ('And the generated act is necessarily in need of a cause.') If what is meant is its need for a cause other than the direct agent and what proceeds from him, that is denied by them. If what is meant is its need for a cause proceeding from the agent, then that does not negate its being his act, for the acts of God Most High proceed from Him by a preponderant factor that comes from Him, yet they are His acts by consensus. And he replied to what he made the second face with what was previously stated in the creation of actions: variation is only in the multiplicity of what is empowered due to multiplicity of power, and in that there is no indication that the act occurs by power. And he replied to the three faces mentioned in the book by the sufficiency of the habit of action. And you may say: It is possible that the occurrence of repulsion is according to intention by way of creation as a matter of habit, and likewise the variation in carrying according to difference in power, so the claim of necessity and its confirmation are not valid.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a response to objections against the theory of generated acts (al-tawleed) in kalām. The commentator presents al-Amidi's replies to three objections (wujuh). The first objection is that generated acts cannot be attributed to the agent because they occur after his incapacity or death. Al-Amidi replies that even if we concede that direct acts are by power, generated acts are not conceivable by power since they occur long after the agent is gone. Moreover, even if they occur according to his intention, that does not make them his acts because direct acts require independent power, while generated acts need a cause. A marginal note then clarifies that occurrence after death does not negate being according to intention if it is from the agent by way of causing the cause. The second objection is about variation in capacities; the reply is that variation only indicates multiplicity of what is empowered, not that the act occurs by power. The third objection is answered by the sufficiency of habit (kifāyat ijrā' al-'ādah): God habitually creates the generated act after the direct act, so the commands, prohibitions, praise, and blame are valid based on that habit. The commentator adds that the claim of necessity (ḍarūra) is baseless.

Key Terms
العادة
القدرة
الاختلاف
الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Amidi, what accounts for the variation in what people can carry (e.g., some carry heavy loads, others light)?
A) Direct power over the act of carrying
B) Multiplicity of power
C) The agent's intention
D) The habit of God

Key Takeaway
The habit of God is sufficient to explain the connection between direct acts and generated acts, and the claim of necessity for direct power is unfounded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish a difficult task, do you truly attribute the result to God's habit of creating the outcome, or do you feel a sense of self-sufficiency? Take a moment to repent of any hidden pride in your own power.
?
You assume that your actions directly produce their effects—that your study causes understanding, your work causes income. Al-Amidi says this necessity is invalid. How does this truth expose your false sense of control? What would change if you lived aware that every effect is directly from God's habit?
?
The objection claimed necessity; al-Amidi replied it is possible that all generated acts are by God's habit. Are you willing to live as if this is true—to stop fearing the breakdown of 'natural' causes and instead trust the One who habitually creates every outcome?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Direct Cause Cannot Be in the Same Locus as the Generated Act
Mu'tazila proof for the impossibility of a direct cause in the same locus as generated act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's claim that a cause powered by originated power cannot occur directly in the same locus as the generated act, and the proof based on impossibility of combining two similars.

Core Idea
A direct act and a generated act cannot coexist because they are similars, and similars cannot combine in one locus.

The Mu'tazila assert a key principle: the cause of a generated act cannot itself be direct in the same place. They argue that if it were allowed, a direct act and a generated act would coexist, which is impossible because they are like two similar things. This passage lays out their reasoning, including a striking analogy about an ant carrying a mountain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and consensus
Read the first part of the passage to find the claim that the cause cannot be direct in the same locus, and note that this is a consensus among the Mu'tazila.
The term 'مباشراً' refers to a direct act, as opposed to a generated (متولد) act.
2
Follow the logical proof
The passage argues that if the cause were direct, a direct act and a generated act would coexist. Since they are similar acts (both movements, for example), they would be two similars in one locus, which is impossible.
The argument relies on the principle that two similars cannot occupy the same singular locus.
3
Reflect on the ant-mountain example
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: allowing the cause to be direct would lead to the absurdity of an ant carrying a mountain. This illustrates the impossibility.
The example shows the extreme consequences of violating the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الأول ( من تلك الفروع (أن السبب المقدور بالقدرة الحادثة يمتنع ) باتفاق المعتزلة ( أن يقع مباشراً في نفس المحل الذي وقع فيه المتولد من غير توسط السبب وإلا لجاز اجتماع مباشر ومتولد في محل واحد ) ، وذلك لأن وجوده فيه لوجود سببه ممكن بلا ريبة والمفروض أنه يمكن وقوعه فيه مباشراً، فقد جاز وجودهما فيه مع اتحاد القدرة المؤثرة فيهما، وهما مثلان واجتماع المثلين محال مع أنه يفضي إلى جواز حمل الذرة للجبل العظيم بأن يحصل فيه أي في الجبل من قدرة الذرة أعدادا من الحمل موازية لأعداد أجزائه فيرتفع الجبل (بها)، أي بتلك الأعداد من الحمل ) وذلك محال ضرورة والجواب أنه ) أي القول بامتناع اجتماعهما ) يناقض أصلكم في جواز اجتماع المثلين) في محل ،واحد فإن المعتزلة جوزوا اجتماعهما مطلقا إلا شرذمة منهم فإنهم فضلوا وقالوا : لا يجوز الاجتماع بين حركتين متماثلتين، ويجوز في غيرهما كما مر في المرصد الرابع من الموقف الثاني ( ثم) نقول : ليس يلزم من تجويز المباشرة ( في غير ما وقع التوليد ( مشروطاً ( بشرط عدم السبب ) ، كما أن وقوعه تولداً مشروط بوجوده ( فلا يلزم اجتماع المثلين لامتناع اجتماع شرطهما بل يكون وقوع كل من المباشرة والتوليد بدلاً عن الآخر، ويحتمل الكلام وجهاً آخر وهو أن تأثيره بالمباشرة في عين ما وقع بالتولد لا في غيره وذلك التأثير على سبيل البدل لما ذكر لئلا يلزم اجتماع تأثيرين على شيء واحد بعينه وهذا الوجه هو المفهوم من أبكار الأفكار والموافق لذكر لفظة العين. قوله : ( واجتماع المثلين (محال إذا كان مبنى الدليل هذه الاستحالة لم يكن له اختصاص بالقدرة الحادثة كما يتبادر من سياق كلامه بل يجري في أفعال الله تعالى على القول بالتوليد فيها . قوله : ( وذلك محال ضرورة وجوابه منع استحالته، نعم هو خلاف ما جرى عليه عادة الله سبحانه وتعالى على أن اجتماع المثلين غير صدورهما من واحد ، فتأمل . قوله : ( ويحتمل الكلام وجهاً آخر الفرق بين الوجهين ظاهر فإن المباشر والمتولد في الوجه الأول متغايران بالشخص يقع المباشر عند عدم السبب ويقع المتولد عند وجوده وفي الوجه الثاني يتحدان به بالذات، يقع تارة بالمباشرة وأخرى بالتوليد ولا يخفى أن لفظ العين في الوجه الثاني على ظاهره وفي الوجه الأول محمول على المثلية أو أريد به العينية بحسب الماهية نظيره أن يقال : زيد عين عمرو في الماهية أي ماهيتهما متحدة .
The first branch of those branches: that the cause that is empowered by the originated power is impossible, by consensus of the Mu'tazila, to occur directly in the very locus in which the generated act occurred without the mediation of the cause, for otherwise it would be possible for a direct act and a generated act to combine in one locus. That is because its existence in it due to the existence of its cause is possible without doubt, and it is assumed that it can occur in it directly, so both have been allowed to exist in it with the unity of the effective power in them, and they are two similars, and the combination of two similars is impossible, even though it leads to the possibility of the ant carrying the great mountain, by there occurring in it, i.e., in the mountain, from the power of the ant, numbers of carrying equal to the numbers of its parts, so the mountain rises by them, i.e., by those numbers of carrying, and that is impossible by necessity. And the answer is that it, i.e., the statement of the impossibility of their combination, contradicts your principle of the permissibility of combining two similars in one locus. For the Mu'tazila permitted their combination absolutely, except a few of them who specified and said: the combination of two similar movements is not permissible, but it is permissible in other than them, as was stated in the fourth observation of the second station. Then we say: Permission of the direct act does not necessitate [what you claim] because the direct act is in a place other than where the generation occurred, conditional upon the absence of the cause, just as its occurrence as generation is conditional upon its existence, so the combination of two similars does not follow due to the impossibility of the combination of their conditions. Rather, the occurrence of each of the direct act and the generation is as an alternative to the other. And the statement can bear another interpretation: that its effect by directness is in the very thing that occurred by generation, not in another, and that effect is by way of alternation as mentioned, to avoid the combination of two effects on one identical thing. This interpretation is what is understood from the work 'Abkār al-Afkār' and is in agreement with the mention of the word 'ayn (identical). His statement: ('And the combination of two similars is impossible'): if the proof is based on this impossibility, it is not specific to originated power as might be understood from the context of his speech, but rather applies to the acts of God Most High according to the view on generation in them. His statement: ('And that is impossible by necessity'): and the answer is to deny its impossibility; rather, it is contrary to what God's custom has been upon, and the combination of two similars is not the same as their proceeding from one, so reflect. His statement: ('And the statement can bear another interpretation'): the difference between the two interpretations is clear. In the first interpretation, the direct act and the generated act are numerically different; the direct act occurs when the cause is absent, and the generated act occurs when it is present. In the second interpretation, they are identical in essence; it occurs sometimes as direct and sometimes as generated. And it is obvious that the word 'ayn in the second interpretation is literal, and in the first interpretation it is metaphorical for similarity, or what is meant is identity in terms of essence, similar to saying: 'Zayd is the 'ayn of 'Amr in essence,' meaning their essences are united.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the first branch (far') of the theory of generation (al-tawleed). The branch states that the cause (sabab) that is empowered by originated power cannot, by consensus of the Mu'tazila, occur as a direct act in the same locus where the generated act occurs without the mediation of the cause. The reason given is that if it were allowed, a direct act and a generated act could combine in one locus, and since both are similar acts, the combination of two similars is impossible. Moreover, it would lead to absurdities like the ant carrying a great mountain. The response is that this contradicts the Mu'tazila's own principle that two similars can combine in one locus, except for a few who restrict it to movements. Additionally, the direct act and the generated act do not actually combine because the direct act is conditional on the absence of the cause, while the generated act is conditional on its presence, so they are alternatives. Another interpretation is that the direct act and the generated act are the same entity but occur in different modes (direct vs. generated) by way of alternation, avoiding the combination of two effects on one thing. The marginal comments clarify that the impossibility of combining similars is not specific to originated power, and that the answer to the claim of necessity is to deny the impossibility — it is only against God's custom. The difference between the two interpretations is explained: in the first, the direct act and generated act are different individuals; in the second, they are identical in essence.

المتولد
المباشر
المحل
اجتماع المثلين
القدرة الحادثة

A) Because the cause and effect must be in different times
B) Because it would lead to the combination of two similars in one locus
C) Because the generated act is not an effect
D) Because the Mu'tazila disagree among themselves

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that a cause cannot be direct in the same locus as its generated effect because that would require two similar acts to coexist, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you strive in worship, do you ever assume that your effort alone can produce multiple spiritual effects simultaneously? How does this passage remind you that your power is limited and that results depend on Allah's custom?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that two similars cannot coexist in one locus. In your heart, can sincere devotion and hidden pride truly coexist? What does this principle reveal about the purity needed in your intentions?
?
If an ant cannot carry a mountain by its own generated acts, what does that tell you about areas in your life where you rely on your own strength to produce outcomes? Where have you assumed that your direct actions guarantee specific results, ignoring the divine custom?

Causality and Created Things

The Response and Two Interpretations
Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazila proof and two interpretations of the relation between direct and generated acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazila argument, including the contradiction with their own principle and the two alternative interpretations of how direct and generated acts relate.

Core Idea
The direct act and generated act are alternatives, not simultaneous; they can also be the same thing under different descriptions.

The Mu'tazila argument is met with a sharp response: it contradicts their own allowance of combining similars. Then the author presents two interpretations—either the acts are numerically distinct but mutually exclusive, or they are identical in essence but occur in different modes. Marginal notes clarify the scope and meaning of 'impossible'.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari response
The response points out that the Mu'tazila's own principle permits combining two similars, so the proof backfires.
Except for a few Mu'tazila who restrict the combination for movements.
2
Understand the first interpretation
The direct act and generated act are in different loci or are conditional on the absence/presence of the cause, so they do not actually coexist.
They are alternatives: one occurs when the cause is absent, the other when present.
3
Study the second interpretation
The direct act and generated act are the very same entity, but it occurs in different modes (direct or generated) by alternation.
This interpretation avoids two effects on one thing.
4
Reflect on the marginal comments
The marginal notes clarify that the impossibility of combining similars is not a logical necessity but only contrary to divine custom, and that the two interpretations differ on numerical vs. essential identity.
These comments deepen the analysis.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الأول ( من تلك الفروع (أن السبب المقدور بالقدرة الحادثة يمتنع ) باتفاق المعتزلة ( أن يقع مباشراً في نفس المحل الذي وقع فيه المتولد من غير توسط السبب وإلا لجاز اجتماع مباشر ومتولد في محل واحد ) ، وذلك لأن وجوده فيه لوجود سببه ممكن بلا ريبة والمفروض أنه يمكن وقوعه فيه مباشراً، فقد جاز وجودهما فيه مع اتحاد القدرة المؤثرة فيهما، وهما مثلان واجتماع المثلين محال مع أنه يفضي إلى جواز حمل الذرة للجبل العظيم بأن يحصل فيه أي في الجبل من قدرة الذرة أعدادا من الحمل موازية لأعداد أجزائه فيرتفع الجبل (بها)، أي بتلك الأعداد من الحمل ) وذلك محال ضرورة والجواب أنه ) أي القول بامتناع اجتماعهما ) يناقض أصلكم في جواز اجتماع المثلين) في محل ،واحد فإن المعتزلة جوزوا اجتماعهما مطلقا إلا شرذمة منهم فإنهم فضلوا وقالوا : لا يجوز الاجتماع بين حركتين متماثلتين، ويجوز في غيرهما كما مر في المرصد الرابع من الموقف الثاني ( ثم) نقول : ليس يلزم من تجويز المباشرة ( في غير ما وقع التوليد ( مشروطاً ( بشرط عدم السبب ) ، كما أن وقوعه تولداً مشروط بوجوده ( فلا يلزم اجتماع المثلين لامتناع اجتماع شرطهما بل يكون وقوع كل من المباشرة والتوليد بدلاً عن الآخر، ويحتمل الكلام وجهاً آخر وهو أن تأثيره بالمباشرة في عين ما وقع بالتولد لا في غيره وذلك التأثير على سبيل البدل لما ذكر لئلا يلزم اجتماع تأثيرين على شيء واحد بعينه وهذا الوجه هو المفهوم من أبكار الأفكار والموافق لذكر لفظة العين. قوله : ( واجتماع المثلين (محال إذا كان مبنى الدليل هذه الاستحالة لم يكن له اختصاص بالقدرة الحادثة كما يتبادر من سياق كلامه بل يجري في أفعال الله تعالى على القول بالتوليد فيها . قوله : ( وذلك محال ضرورة وجوابه منع استحالته، نعم هو خلاف ما جرى عليه عادة الله سبحانه وتعالى على أن اجتماع المثلين غير صدورهما من واحد ، فتأمل . قوله : ( ويحتمل الكلام وجهاً آخر الفرق بين الوجهين ظاهر فإن المباشر والمتولد في الوجه الأول متغايران بالشخص يقع المباشر عند عدم السبب ويقع المتولد عند وجوده وفي الوجه الثاني يتحدان به بالذات، يقع تارة بالمباشرة وأخرى بالتوليد ولا يخفى أن لفظ العين في الوجه الثاني على ظاهره وفي الوجه الأول محمول على المثلية أو أريد به العينية بحسب الماهية نظيره أن يقال : زيد عين عمرو في الماهية أي ماهيتهما متحدة .
The first branch of those branches: that the cause that is empowered by the originated power is impossible, by consensus of the Mu'tazila, to occur directly in the very locus in which the generated act occurred without the mediation of the cause, for otherwise it would be possible for a direct act and a generated act to combine in one locus. That is because its existence in it due to the existence of its cause is possible without doubt, and it is assumed that it can occur in it directly, so both have been allowed to exist in it with the unity of the effective power in them, and they are two similars, and the combination of two similars is impossible, even though it leads to the possibility of the ant carrying the great mountain, by there occurring in it, i.e., in the mountain, from the power of the ant, numbers of carrying equal to the numbers of its parts, so the mountain rises by them, i.e., by those numbers of carrying, and that is impossible by necessity. And the answer is that it, i.e., the statement of the impossibility of their combination, contradicts your principle of the permissibility of combining two similars in one locus. For the Mu'tazila permitted their combination absolutely, except a few of them who specified and said: the combination of two similar movements is not permissible, but it is permissible in other than them, as was stated in the fourth observation of the second station. Then we say: Permission of the direct act does not necessitate [what you claim] because the direct act is in a place other than where the generation occurred, conditional upon the absence of the cause, just as its occurrence as generation is conditional upon its existence, so the combination of two similars does not follow due to the impossibility of the combination of their conditions. Rather, the occurrence of each of the direct act and the generation is as an alternative to the other. And the statement can bear another interpretation: that its effect by directness is in the very thing that occurred by generation, not in another, and that effect is by way of alternation as mentioned, to avoid the combination of two effects on one identical thing. This interpretation is what is understood from the work 'Abkār al-Afkār' and is in agreement with the mention of the word 'ayn (identical). His statement: ('And the combination of two similars is impossible'): if the proof is based on this impossibility, it is not specific to originated power as might be understood from the context of his speech, but rather applies to the acts of God Most High according to the view on generation in them. His statement: ('And that is impossible by necessity'): and the answer is to deny its impossibility; rather, it is contrary to what God's custom has been upon, and the combination of two similars is not the same as their proceeding from one, so reflect. His statement: ('And the statement can bear another interpretation'): the difference between the two interpretations is clear. In the first interpretation, the direct act and the generated act are numerically different; the direct act occurs when the cause is absent, and the generated act occurs when it is present. In the second interpretation, they are identical in essence; it occurs sometimes as direct and sometimes as generated. And it is obvious that the word 'ayn in the second interpretation is literal, and in the first interpretation it is metaphorical for similarity, or what is meant is identity in terms of essence, similar to saying: 'Zayd is the 'ayn of 'Amr in essence,' meaning their essences are united.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the first branch (far') of the theory of generation (al-tawleed). The branch states that the cause (sabab) that is empowered by originated power cannot, by consensus of the Mu'tazila, occur as a direct act in the same locus where the generated act occurs without the mediation of the cause. The reason given is that if it were allowed, a direct act and a generated act could combine in one locus, and since both are similar acts, the combination of two similars is impossible. Moreover, it would lead to absurdities like the ant carrying a great mountain. The response is that this contradicts the Mu'tazila's own principle that two similars can combine in one locus, except for a few who restrict it to movements. Additionally, the direct act and the generated act do not actually combine because the direct act is conditional on the absence of the cause, while the generated act is conditional on its presence, so they are alternatives. Another interpretation is that the direct act and the generated act are the same entity but occur in different modes (direct vs. generated) by way of alternation, avoiding the combination of two effects on one thing. The marginal comments clarify that the impossibility of combining similars is not specific to originated power, and that the answer to the claim of necessity is to deny the impossibility — it is only against God's custom. The difference between the two interpretations is explained: in the first, the direct act and generated act are different individuals; in the second, they are identical in essence.

Key Terms
اجتماع المثلين
البدل
العين
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the two interpretations contradict each other or that the impossibility of combining similars is a logical necessity.
Correction: The interpretations address different levels: numerical identity vs. essential identity. The impossibility is not a necessary truth but only contrary to divine custom, as clarified in the margins.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between the first and second interpretations of the direct act and generated act?
A) The first says they are the same act; the second says they are different.
B) The first says they are numerically different; the second says they are identical in essence.
C) The first says they are both impossible; the second says they are both possible.
D) The first relies on causality; the second on divine custom.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response shows that the direct and generated acts do not actually combine: they are either mutually exclusive alternatives or the same entity under different descriptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you cause harm indirectly, do you consider yourself responsible or do you blame circumstances? This lesson shows that in Ash'ari thought, the direct and generated act are either alternatives or identical. What does that mean for your accountability for unintended consequences? Are you willing to see your actions and their effects as inseparable before God?
?
This lesson argues that the impossibility of combining two similars is not a necessary truth but only God's custom. How does this affect your view of miracles? Are you relying on a deterministic view of causality that limits God's power? Reflect on a time you assumed something was impossible but God could have done it.
?
If the direct act and generated act are alternatives or identical, then when you act, you are not the ultimate cause; God's custom allows either. How does this affect your reliance on means or on God? In what area of your life do you trust the means more than the One who establishes the custom?

The Purpose of Discussing Branches After Invalidation
Al-Jurjani justifies discussing generation branches after invalidating their root.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani mentions the branches of generation theory even after arguing that their root is invalid.

Core Idea
Invalidating the root does not erase the need to expose confusion.

The author has just argued that the theory of generation is invalid. One might expect him to ignore its branches. Instead, he chooses to discuss them. This lesson explains his motivation: to highlight the contradictions within the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style in stating the principle.
2
Identify the logical move
The author states that invalidating the principle (أصل التوليد) makes the branches (فروع) irrelevant. But he then says he will mention them anyway. Identify why he gives this reason.
3
Reflect on the purpose
The purpose is not to prove the branches but to expose the confusion (اضطراب) and contradiction (تنافي) in the opponents' views. Why is this a valid pedagogical move?
This connects to the broader method of refutation in kalam.

Source Text

المرصد السادس - المقصد الثاني : في التوليد وفروعه. اختلاف القدر فلا يصح دعوى الضرورة وتأييدها، ( ولما أبطلنا أصل التوليد بطل ما هو متفرع عليه ) فلا حاجة إلى ذكر فروعه والجواب عنها ( لكنا نذكرها تنبيهاً على ما وقع في آرائهم من الاضطراب والتنافي . (الفرع الأول من تلك الفروع أن السبب المقدور بالقدرة الحادثة يمتنع ) باتفاق المعتزلة ( أن يقع مباشراً بالمتولد من القدرة الحادثة من غير توسط السبب وإلا لجاز اجتماع مباشر ومتولد في محل واحد ) ، وذلك لأن وجوده فيه لوجود سببه ممكن بلا ريبة والمفروض أنه يمكن وقوعه فيه مباشراً، فقد جاز وجودهما فيه مع اتحاد القدرة المؤثرة فيهما، وهما مثلان واجتماع المثلين محال مع أنه يفضي إلى جواز حمل الذرة للجبل العظيم بأن يحصل فيه أي في الجبل من قدرة الذرة أعدادا من الحمل موازية لأعداد أجزائه فيرتفع الجبل (بها)، أي بتلك الأعداد من الحمل ) وذلك محال ضرورة والجواب أنه ) أي القول بامتناع اجتماعهما ) يناقض أصلكم في جواز اجتماع المثلين) في محل واحد فإن المعتزلة جوزوا اجتماعهما مطلقا إلا شرذمة منهم فإنهم فضلوا وقالوا : لا يجوز الاجتماع بين حركتين متماثلتين، ويجوز في غيرهما كما مر في المرصد الرابع من الموقف الثاني ( ثم) نقول : ليس يلزم من تجويز المباشرة ( في غير ما وقع التوليد ( مشروطاً ( بشرط عدم السبب ) ، كما أن وقوعه تولداً مشروط بوجوده ( فلا يلزم اجتماع المثلين لامتناع اجتماع شرطهما بل يكون وقوع كل من المباشرة والتوليد بدلاً عن الآخر، ويحتمل الكلام وجهاً آخر وهو أن تأثيره بالمباشرة في عين ما وقع بالتولد لا في غيره وذلك التأثير على سبيل البدل لما ذكر لئلا يلزم اجتماع تأثيرين على شيء واحد بعينه وهذا الوجه هو المفهوم من أبكار الأفكار والموافق لذكر لفظة العين. قوله : ( واجتماع المثلين (محال إذا كان مبنى الدليل هذه الاستحالة لم يكن له اختصاص بالقدرة الحادثة كما يتبادر من سياق كلامه بل يجري في أفعال الله تعالى على القول بالتوليد فيها . قوله : ( وذلك محال ضرورة وجوابه منع استحالته، نعم هو خلاف ما جرى عليه عادة الله سبحانه وتعالى على أن اجتماع المثلين غير صدورهما من واحد ، فتأمل . قوله : ( ويحتمل الكلام وجهاً آخر الفرق بين الوجهين ظاهر فإن المباشر والمتولد في الوجه الأول متغايران بالشخص يقع المباشر عند عدم السبب ويقع المتولد عند وجوده وفي الوجه الثاني يتحدان به بالذات، يقع تارة بالمباشرة وأخرى بالتوليد ولا يخفى أن لفظ العين في الوجه الثاني على ظاهره وفي الوجه الأول محمول على المثلية أو أريد به العينية بحسب الماهية نظيره أن يقال : زيد عين عمرو في الماهية أي ماهيتهما متحدة .
The Sixth Observatory - The Second Purpose: On Generation and its Branches. Disagreement in predestination so the claim of necessity and its support is invalid. (And when we invalidated the principle of generation, what is branched from it is invalidated) so there is no need to mention its branches and answer them, (but we mention them to alert to what occurred in their opinions of confusion and contradiction). (The first branch of those branches: that the cause brought about by the originated power is impossible), by agreement of the Mu'tazilites, (that it occur directly as the generated from the originated power without the intermediation of the cause; otherwise, the conjunction of a direct and a generated [effect] in one substrate would be permissible), and that is because its existence in it due to the existence of its cause is possible without doubt, and the presupposed is that it is possible for it to occur in it directly, thus their existence together in it with the unity of the effective power in them would be permissible, and they are two similars, and the conjunction of similars is impossible, especially that it leads to the permissibility of the atom carrying the great mountain, by there occurring in it, i.e., in the mountain, from the power of the atom, numbers of carrying corresponding to the numbers of its parts, so the mountain rises (by them), i.e., by those numbers of carrying, and that is impossible by necessity. And the answer: that (i.e., the statement of the impossibility of their conjunction) contradicts your principle of the permissibility of the conjunction of similars in one substrate, for the Mu'tazilites permitted their conjunction absolutely, except a few of them who dissented and said: conjunction is not permissible between two identical motions, but is permissible in others, as passed in the Fourth Observatory of the Second Stand (then) we say: it is not necessary from permitting the direct occurrence (in what does not involve generation, conditioned (by the condition of the absence of the cause), just as its occurrence as generated is conditioned by its existence, so the conjunction of similars does not follow due to the impossibility of the conjunction of their conditions, but rather the occurrence of each of the direct and the generated is alternative to the other, and the speech may have another aspect: that his effect by directness is in the very thing that occurred by generation, not in another, and that effect is by way of substitution for what was mentioned, lest two effects on one and the same thing occur, and this aspect is what is understood from Abkar al-Afkar and is in agreement with the mention of the word 'ayn. He said: "And the conjunction of similars is impossible" – if the basis of the proof is this impossibility, it is not specific to the originated power as may be apparent from the context of his speech, but rather applies to the actions of God Most High according to the theory of generation in them. He said: "And that is impossible by necessity" – and its answer is the denial of its impossibility; rather, it is contrary to what God's custom runs on, but the conjunction of similars is not the same as their emanating from one [agent], so reflect. He said: "And the speech may have another aspect" – the difference between the two aspects is clear: in the first aspect, the direct and the generated are different in person; the direct occurs when the cause is absent, and the generated occurs when it is present. In the second aspect, they unite in essence; it occurs sometimes by directness and sometimes by generation, and it is not hidden that the word 'ayn in the second aspect is literal, and in the first aspect it is carried on similarity or 'ayn is intended by way of essential similarity, analogous to saying: Zayd is the 'ayn of 'Amr in essence, i.e., their essences are identical.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents the first branch of the theory of generation (التوليد). It begins by stating that the Mu'tazilites agree that a cause brought about by created power cannot occur directly without an intermediary cause, otherwise two similars (direct and generated) would coexist in one substrate, which they claim is impossible. The author then gives a reductio ad absurdum: if direct occurrence were possible, an atom could carry a mountain. The answer shows this contradicts the Mu'tazilite allowance of conjunction of similars, unless one restricts it as some did for identical motions. The commentary distinguishes two interpretations: either the direct and generated are different individuals occurring under different conditions, or they are the same essence occurring in two ways. The word 'ayn (عين) is discussed in terms of literal identity versus similarity.

A) To prove that the branches are still valid.

The author discusses invalidated branches not to prove them, but to expose internal contradictions in Mu'tazilite thought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Have you ever defended a position you hold without scrutinizing its internal inconsistencies, assuming that if the core is right, all its implications must be sound? How might Al-Jurjani's approach of highlighting confusion even in invalidated branches challenge your own intellectual habits?
?
In which area of your life are you most reluctant to expose your own contradictions, knowing that it would be uncomfortable? Will you take the time to examine them for the sake of clarity, even if the outcome seems already settled?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

The First Branch: Impossibility of Direct Act
Al-Jurjani presents the first branch and its proof via impossibility of conjunction of similars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first branch of generation theory: that a caused act cannot occur directly by created power, and the proof for it.

Core Idea
A direct act would lead to the impossible conjunction of similars.

Al-Jurjani now takes up the first branch of generation theory. He states the Mu'tazilite position that the caused act cannot be direct. He provides a concise proof based on the impossibility of two similars coexisting in one substrate, and illustrates with a striking example.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and proof
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Focus on the structure: claim, justification, illustrative example.
2
Understand the proof logic
The proof uses a conditional: if the caused act could be direct, then a direct act and a generated act would both exist in one substrate from the same power. They would be 'similars' (مثلان), and the conjunction of similars is impossible. This is the core of the argument.
Similars here means two effects of the same type produced by one power in one place.
3
Examine the reductio
The reductio ad absurdum (atom carrying a mountain) shows the absurdity: if direct act were possible, an atom could generate enough 'carrying' powers to lift a mountain. Why is this considered absurd?
This highlights the violation of observable causal patterns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What is the main argument against the direct occurrence of a caused act?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed a success or failure entirely to your own effort, forgetting the chain of causes Allah has put in place? Read the passage again: if a direct act from your power is impossible, how can you claim credit for anything without acknowledging Allah's mediation? Conclude your reflection with a resolution to thank Allah for every means He provides.
?
The atom-and-mountain analogy shows that your power is so limited that it cannot even lift an atom's weight without a cause. Does your heart truly depend on Allah alone, or do you rely on your skills, wealth, or people as if they were independent causes? What step can you take today to shift your reliance from created causes to the Creator?
?
If the conjunction of two similars from one power is impossible, how does that impact your understanding of tawhid? Can you point to any area of your life where you are 'conjoining' something else with Allah in your trust or love? Write down one action you will take to purify your tawhid.

advanced
