Refining the Argument: Objection and Two Interpretations
Al-Jurjani presents an objection and responds with a conditional and two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite objection to the proof and the two interpretations of how direct and generated acts relate.

Core Idea
The proof must be refined to avoid contradicting Mu'tazilite principles.

The argument for the first branch faces an objection: the impossibility of conjunction of similars contradicts the Mu'tazilite general permissibility of such conjunction. Al-Jurjani responds by noting that the direct and generated acts occur under different conditions, so they do not truly coincide. He then provides two interpretations of their relationship, clarifying the nuances of the term 'ayn (عين).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the full Arabic passage with its translation. Identify the objection: that the proof relies on an impossibility that the Mu'tazilites themselves reject (they allow conjunction of similars). Then find the response.
The response has two parts: a conditional distinction and two interpretations.
2
Understand the conditionality
The response argues that the direct and generated acts occur under opposite conditions (absence vs. presence of the cause). Since conditions cannot coexist, the acts are alternatives, not simultaneous. This avoids the conjunction of similars.
3
Distinguish the two interpretations
The first interpretation: the direct and generated are different individuals occurring under different conditions. The second: they are the same essence occurring in two modes (direct and generated). The word 'ayn (عين) is literal in the second, and metaphorical (essential similarity) in the first.
This shows the depth of the dialectical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثاني : في التوليد وفروعه. اختلاف القدر فلا يصح دعوى الضرورة وتأييدها، ( ولما أبطلنا أصل التوليد بطل ما هو متفرع عليه ) فلا حاجة إلى ذكر فروعه والجواب عنها ( لكنا نذكرها تنبيهاً على ما وقع في آرائهم من الاضطراب والتنافي . (الفرع الأول من تلك الفروع أن السبب المقدور بالقدرة الحادثة يمتنع ) باتفاق المعتزلة ( أن يقع مباشراً بالمتولد من القدرة الحادثة من غير توسط السبب وإلا لجاز اجتماع مباشر ومتولد في محل واحد ) ، وذلك لأن وجوده فيه لوجود سببه ممكن بلا ريبة والمفروض أنه يمكن وقوعه فيه مباشراً، فقد جاز وجودهما فيه مع اتحاد القدرة المؤثرة فيهما، وهما مثلان واجتماع المثلين محال مع أنه يفضي إلى جواز حمل الذرة للجبل العظيم بأن يحصل فيه أي في الجبل من قدرة الذرة أعدادا من الحمل موازية لأعداد أجزائه فيرتفع الجبل (بها)، أي بتلك الأعداد من الحمل ) وذلك محال ضرورة والجواب أنه ) أي القول بامتناع اجتماعهما ) يناقض أصلكم في جواز اجتماع المثلين) في محل واحد فإن المعتزلة جوزوا اجتماعهما مطلقا إلا شرذمة منهم فإنهم فضلوا وقالوا : لا يجوز الاجتماع بين حركتين متماثلتين، ويجوز في غيرهما كما مر في المرصد الرابع من الموقف الثاني ( ثم) نقول : ليس يلزم من تجويز المباشرة ( في غير ما وقع التوليد ( مشروطاً ( بشرط عدم السبب ) ، كما أن وقوعه تولداً مشروط بوجوده ( فلا يلزم اجتماع المثلين لامتناع اجتماع شرطهما بل يكون وقوع كل من المباشرة والتوليد بدلاً عن الآخر، ويحتمل الكلام وجهاً آخر وهو أن تأثيره بالمباشرة في عين ما وقع بالتولد لا في غيره وذلك التأثير على سبيل البدل لما ذكر لئلا يلزم اجتماع تأثيرين على شيء واحد بعينه وهذا الوجه هو المفهوم من أبكار الأفكار والموافق لذكر لفظة العين. قوله : ( واجتماع المثلين (محال إذا كان مبنى الدليل هذه الاستحالة لم يكن له اختصاص بالقدرة الحادثة كما يتبادر من سياق كلامه بل يجري في أفعال الله تعالى على القول بالتوليد فيها . قوله : ( وذلك محال ضرورة وجوابه منع استحالته، نعم هو خلاف ما جرى عليه عادة الله سبحانه وتعالى على أن اجتماع المثلين غير صدورهما من واحد ، فتأمل . قوله : ( ويحتمل الكلام وجهاً آخر الفرق بين الوجهين ظاهر فإن المباشر والمتولد في الوجه الأول متغايران بالشخص يقع المباشر عند عدم السبب ويقع المتولد عند وجوده وفي الوجه الثاني يتحدان به بالذات، يقع تارة بالمباشرة وأخرى بالتوليد ولا يخفى أن لفظ العين في الوجه الثاني على ظاهره وفي الوجه الأول محمول على المثلية أو أريد به العينية بحسب الماهية نظيره أن يقال : زيد عين عمرو في الماهية أي ماهيتهما متحدة .
The Sixth Observatory - The Second Purpose: On Generation and its Branches. Disagreement in predestination so the claim of necessity and its support is invalid. (And when we invalidated the principle of generation, what is branched from it is invalidated) so there is no need to mention its branches and answer them, (but we mention them to alert to what occurred in their opinions of confusion and contradiction). (The first branch of those branches: that the cause brought about by the originated power is impossible), by agreement of the Mu'tazilites, (that it occur directly as the generated from the originated power without the intermediation of the cause; otherwise, the conjunction of a direct and a generated [effect] in one substrate would be permissible), and that is because its existence in it due to the existence of its cause is possible without doubt, and the presupposed is that it is possible for it to occur in it directly, thus their existence together in it with the unity of the effective power in them would be permissible, and they are two similars, and the conjunction of similars is impossible, especially that it leads to the permissibility of the atom carrying the great mountain, by there occurring in it, i.e., in the mountain, from the power of the atom, numbers of carrying corresponding to the numbers of its parts, so the mountain rises (by them), i.e., by those numbers of carrying, and that is impossible by necessity. And the answer: that (i.e., the statement of the impossibility of their conjunction) contradicts your principle of the permissibility of the conjunction of similars in one substrate, for the Mu'tazilites permitted their conjunction absolutely, except a few of them who dissented and said: conjunction is not permissible between two identical motions, but is permissible in others, as passed in the Fourth Observatory of the Second Stand (then) we say: it is not necessary from permitting the direct occurrence (in what does not involve generation, conditioned (by the condition of the absence of the cause), just as its occurrence as generated is conditioned by its existence, so the conjunction of similars does not follow due to the impossibility of the conjunction of their conditions, but rather the occurrence of each of the direct and the generated is alternative to the other, and the speech may have another aspect: that his effect by directness is in the very thing that occurred by generation, not in another, and that effect is by way of substitution for what was mentioned, lest two effects on one and the same thing occur, and this aspect is what is understood from Abkar al-Afkar and is in agreement with the mention of the word 'ayn. He said: "And the conjunction of similars is impossible" – if the basis of the proof is this impossibility, it is not specific to the originated power as may be apparent from the context of his speech, but rather applies to the actions of God Most High according to the theory of generation in them. He said: "And that is impossible by necessity" – and its answer is the denial of its impossibility; rather, it is contrary to what God's custom runs on, but the conjunction of similars is not the same as their emanating from one [agent], so reflect. He said: "And the speech may have another aspect" – the difference between the two aspects is clear: in the first aspect, the direct and the generated are different in person; the direct occurs when the cause is absent, and the generated occurs when it is present. In the second aspect, they unite in essence; it occurs sometimes by directness and sometimes by generation, and it is not hidden that the word 'ayn in the second aspect is literal, and in the first aspect it is carried on similarity or 'ayn is intended by way of essential similarity, analogous to saying: Zayd is the 'ayn of 'Amr in essence, i.e., their essences are identical.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents the first branch of the theory of generation (التوليد). It begins by stating that the Mu'tazilites agree that a cause brought about by created power cannot occur directly without an intermediary cause, otherwise two similars (direct and generated) would coexist in one substrate, which they claim is impossible. The author then gives a reductio ad absurdum: if direct occurrence were possible, an atom could carry a mountain. The answer shows this contradicts the Mu'tazilite allowance of conjunction of similars, unless one restricts it as some did for identical motions. The commentary distinguishes two interpretations: either the direct and generated are different individuals occurring under different conditions, or they are the same essence occurring in two ways. The word 'ayn (عين) is discussed in terms of literal identity versus similarity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof against direct act is absolute and applies only to created power.
Correction: The response shows the proof is conditional on the Mu'tazilite principle, and if based on impossibility of similars, it applies even to divine actions. The absurdity is not absolute but contrary to custom.

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation, how do the direct and generated acts relate?
A) They are the same act occurring at different times.
B) They are different individuals occurring under different conditions (absence vs. presence of cause).
C) They are both caused by the same power in the same substrate simultaneously.
D) They are impossible by divine custom.

Key Takeaway
The proof against direct act is refined through conditional alternatives and two interpretations, showing the dialectical depth of kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even a logical proof must be examined for hidden assumptions and conditional contexts. In your own beliefs about causality and divine action, are there unexamined assumptions you hold as absolute? Will you take time today to question one such assumption in light of this method?
?
The passage explains that the impossibility of conjunction of similars is only contrary to God's custom, not absolutely impossible. How often do you mistake what is merely customary or familiar for what is necessary? Consider one area of your life where you treat a pattern as fixed—are you open to the possibility that God can operate beyond it?
?
The two interpretations present a choice: the act can be either two different individuals or one essence with two modes. Does your understanding of divine actions (e.g., how God creates or responds) tend to oversimplify? Will you humbly accept the complexity of theological discourse rather than demanding simple answers?

The Fourth Branch: Sounds and Pains – Claim and Response
Claim and response on generation of sounds and pains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that sounds and pains are generated and the Ash'ari response that the so-called causes are merely conditions for direct divine action.

Core Idea
Sounds and pains need not be caused; they can be conditions for God's direct power.

This lesson examines the fourth branch of the generation discussion. The passage first argues that sounds and pains from human actions must occur through generation (tawleed), because they depend on physical contact. Then the response rejects necessary causation, suggesting that these physical factors are only conditions (shurut) for direct divine creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: claim, then response.
The response begins with 'والجواب'.
2
Identify the claim for generation
Find the sentence stating that sounds and pains only occur through generation and note the reasoning based on physical causes.
The claim uses 'لا يعقل' to assert inconceivability.
3
Analyze the response
The response denies that those factors are causes (asbab) and suggests they are mere conditions. Consider why this preserves divine omnipotence.
This is a key Ash'ari move against natural necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الرابع) من تلك الفروع (الأصوات والآلام الحاصلة بفعل الآدميين لا تحصل إلا بالتوليد، إذ لا يعقل وجود صوت إلا باعتمادات لبعض الأجرام على بعض واصطكاك بينها وكذا الحال في الألم الحاصل من الآدمي فلو كانت هذه الأمور واقعة بطريق المباشرة لما توقفت على هذ الأسباب. والجواب : لا نسلم أنها أسباب، بل جاز أن تكون شروطاً لوقوعها من القدرة مباشرة، وزاد أبو هاشم التأليفات) على الإطلاق لتوقفها على المجاورة فتكون متولدة منها، وجوابه ما عرفت آنفاً، ( ومنعه أبو علي في التأليف القائم بجسمين هما أو أحدهما محل القدرة كمن ضم أصبعه إلى أصبعه أو ) ضم أصبعه ( إلى جسم (آخر) وقال : هذا التأليف يقع بغير توليد (بخلاف التأليف القائم بمحلين غير محل القدرة، كجسمين مباينين لمحلها فإنه لا يقع بغير التوليد لأن الفعل الصادر عن العباد في محل خارج بتمامه عن محل قدرتهم لا يكون مباشراً بالاتفاق بين القائلين بالتوليد .
(The fourth) of those branches: (Sounds and pains resulting from the action of human beings do not occur except by generation, for it is not conceivable that a sound exists except by the pressing of some bodies upon others and their collision, and similarly for the pain resulting from the human. So if these things occurred by way of direct action, they would not depend on these causes. And the answer: We do not grant that they are causes; rather, it is possible that they are conditions for their occurrence by direct power. And Abu Hashim added compositions absolutely, because they depend on contiguity, so they are generated from it, and his answer is what you already know. And Abu Ali denied it in the case of composition subsisting in two bodies, both or one of them being the locus of power, such as when one presses his finger to his finger or presses his finger to another body, and said: This composition occurs without generation, unlike the composition subsisting in two loci that are not loci of power, such as two bodies separate from its locus, for it does not occur without generation, because the act emanating from servants in a locus entirely external to the locus of their power is not direct by consensus among those who affirm generation.)
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth branch (al-far' al-rabi') of the discussion on generation. It states the claim: sounds (aswat) and pains (alam) resulting from human actions only occur through generation (tawleed). The reason given is that it is inconceivable for a sound to exist without the pressing (i'timadat) of some bodies upon others and their collision (istiksak), and similarly for pain. If these occurred directly (mubashara), they would not depend on such causes. The response denies that these are causes (asbab); rather, they may be conditions (shurut) for their occurrence by direct power. Then the commentator mentions that Abu Hashim extended this to compositions (ta'lifat) absolutely, because they depend on contiguity (mujawara), so they are generated. But his answer is the same. Abu Ali (al-Jubba'i) restricted this: in the case of composition subsisting in two bodies that are both or one within the locus of the servant's power (e.g., pressing one's finger to another or to a body), it occurs without generation. However, composition in two loci entirely outside the locus of power (e.g., two separate bodies) cannot occur without generation, because an act in a locus entirely external to the locus of power is not direct by consensus. The passage follows the order: claim, response, addition by Abu Hashim, restriction by Abu Ali.

Key Terms
الأصوات
الآلام
التوليد
المباشرة
الأسباب
الشروط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if sounds depend on physical causes, they must be generated necessarily.
Correction: The response shows that God can create sounds directly with those physical factors as mere conditions, not causes.

Checkpoint
According to the response, the factors mentioned (pressing, collision) are:
A) Necessary causes.
B) Mere conditions.
C) Irrelevant.
D) The only way sounds occur.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school denies necessary causation; what appear as causes may be conditions for God's direct action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain from a physical injury, do you instinctively see it as a direct act of God or as a natural consequence of the injury? What would change in your response if you truly believed it is directly from God?
?
If sounds and pains are not generated by causes but are directly created by God, how does that affect your understanding of responsibility for what you hear and feel? Are you more or less accountable for your reactions?
?
Consider a moment when you felt pain and immediately sought a physical remedy. How does the lesson's teaching that the remedy is only a condition, not a cause, challenge your reliance on material means? Will you now seek God's direct help with greater urgency?

The Debate on Compositions: Abu Hashim vs Abu Ali
Debate on whether compositions require generation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different rulings on whether compositions require generation, depending on the locus of power.

Core Idea
Not all compositions are generated; it depends on whether the locus of action is within the servant's power.

Abu Hashim extended generation to all compositions because they depend on contiguity. Abu Ali restricted this: when the composition occurs in the servant's own body parts (within the locus of power), it is direct; only compositions entirely outside the locus require generation. This lesson explores their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on compositions
Read the Arabic and translation. Note three parts: Abu Hashim's claim, his answer, and Abu Ali's restriction with examples.
The passage builds on the earlier response.
2
Identify Abu Hashim's position
Find the claim that all compositions are generated because they depend on contiguity. Note how his answer refers back.
Abu Hashim says 'فتكون متولدة منها'.
3
Identify Abu Ali's distinction
Find where Abu Ali denies generation for compositions in the locus of power (e.g., pressing finger to finger) but affirms it for those outside.
Note the example with two separate bodies.
4
Understand the rationale
Reflect on why acts in an external locus cannot be direct by consensus among those who affirm generation.
The key is the locus of power.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Answers to Objections on Tawleed and Shubha
Two answers to objections about tawleed being hindered by shubha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's responses to two objections regarding the impact of doubt on the generation of knowledge from recollection.

Core Idea
Doubt only blocks generation when present; its absence allows creation.

Sanusi addresses two objections concerning tawleed (generation) when a doubt (shubha) arises. The first refutes the premise that obligation applies only to actions created by humans. The second argues that generation is only prevented when doubt is actually present, not when it is absent. This lesson unpacks these responses and the further objection about divine vs. human acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: first answer, second answer, then a further objection and response.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the first answer
The first answer denies the premise that obligation only applies to what is within human power. This builds on a prior refutation about the creation of actions.
Refer back to the issue of 'خلق الأعمال' (creation of actions) for context.
3
Analyze the second answer
The second answer has two parts: first, a denial that doubt can occur with recollection of sound consideration; second, a conditional concession that even if it does, generation is only prevented when doubt is present.
Notice the distinction between presence and absence of doubt.
4
Examine the further objection and response
The objector argues that since doubt is human and recollection is divine, preventing generation implies human act repels divine act. Sanusi responds with an analogy of a strong object moved by wind.
The analogy shows that the human act (holding) does not negate the divine act (movement).
5
Reflect on the key principle
The core principle is that generation (tawleed) is only blocked by the actual presence of doubt, not by its possibility or absence. This preserves the causal role of recollection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجواب الأول مبني على أن التكليف لا يكون إلا بما هو مقدور للعبد ومخلوق له وقد بينا بطلانه في مسألة خلق الأعمال وجواب الثاني: لا نسلم إمكان عروض الشبهة مع تذكر النظر الصحيح وكلامنا فيه ولا يمتنع التوليد عند عدمها كما في ابتداء النظر، أي وإن سلمنا إمكان عروض الشبهة عند تذكر النظر الصحيح فذلك يمنع توليد التذكر عند عروض الشبهة ولا يمنع توليده عند عدمها كما في ابتداء النظر فإن عروض الشبهة يمنع توليده ولا يمنع ذلك توليده حال عدمها فإن قيل : الشبهة من فعل العبد والتذكر من فعل الله فيلزم من منع الشبهة توليده دفع فعل العبد لفعل الله وذلك باطل بخلاف دفع الشبهة توليد ابتداء النظر الذي هو فعل العبد أيضاً، قلنا : يلزمكم مثله في إمساك الأيدي القوي الشيء الذي يتحرك عند الرياح العاصفة عليه من أن تحركه تلك الرياح سواء كانت تحرك ذلك الشيء فعلاً مباشراً للرب أو متولداً من فعله الذي هو حركة الرياح فما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا.
The first answer is based on the fact that obligation is only with respect to what is within the servant's power and created by him, and we have already shown its invalidity in the issue of the creation of actions. And the second answer: we do not grant the possibility of the occurrence of a doubt (shubha) together with the recollection of sound consideration (tadhakkur al-nazar al-sahih), and our discussion is about it; and generation (tawleed) is not prevented in its absence, as in the beginning of consideration. That is, even if we grant the possibility of a doubt occurring during the recollection of sound consideration, that prevents the generation of recollection at the occurrence of the doubt, but does not prevent its generation when it is absent, as in the beginning of consideration. For the occurrence of a doubt prevents its generation, but that does not prevent its generation in the absence of the doubt. If it is said: the doubt is an act of the servant and the recollection is an act of God, so the prevention of its generation by the doubt entails the repulsion of the servant's act of God, which is false, unlike the prevention of the generation of the beginning of consideration by the doubt, which is also an act of the servant. We say: the same applies to you in the case of holding back a strong object that moves when strong winds blow upon it, regarding its movement by those winds—whether that movement is a direct act of the Lord or generated from His act which is the movement of the wind. So whatever your answer is, it is our answer.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents two responses to objections concerning tawleed (generation) when a doubt (shubha) arises. The first answer denies the premise that obligation only applies to what is within human power and created by humans, referencing a prior refutation in the issue of the creation of actions. The second answer argues that a doubt does not prevent generation when it is absent; generation is only blocked when the doubt is present, not when it is absent, analogous to the beginning of consideration. The text then considers a further objection: since the doubt is the servant's act and recollection is God's act, preventing generation by the doubt would mean the servant's act repels God's act, which is false. The response uses an analogy: just as holding back a strong object moved by wind does not negate the wind's role, similarly the doubt does not negate generation. The argument proceeds in objection-and-answer format, maintaining that generation remains valid when no doubt exists.

Key Terms
التوليد (tawleed)
الشبهة (shubha)
التذكر (tadhakkur)
النظر (nazar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any doubt automatically blocks the generation of knowledge from recollection.
Correction: The passage clarifies that generation is only prevented when doubt is actually present, not when it is absent or merely possible.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second objection, when does the recollection of sound consideration generate knowledge?
A) Only when no doubt is present
B) Always, regardless of doubt
C) Only when a doubt is present
D) Never, because doubt always interferes

Key Takeaway
The generation of knowledge from recollection is not hindered by doubt when the doubt is absent; only the actual presence of doubt prevents it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a doubt about a core belief of Islam enters your mind, do you immediately recall the sound arguments you once knew, or does the doubt paralyze you? What does this lesson teach you about the role of the doubt's presence versus its absence in your ability to recollect truth?
?
In your own experience, have you ever assumed that a single doubt invalidates all your previous certainties? How does the distinction between 'doubt present' and 'doubt absent' change how you should respond when doubts arise?
?
The analogy of holding back an object from wind shows that you can still choose to release it. When a doubt holds you back, what is your personal responsibility to remove that doubt so that the generation of recollection can occur? Will you actively seek to clear the doubt, or passively accept it as permanent?

Sounds and Pains: Generated or Direct?
Argument that sounds and pains from human actions occur only by tawleed, with responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that sounds and pains from human actions occur only by generation (tawleed), the response that physical interactions are conditions rather than causes, and the extension of this debate to compositions (taʾlīfāt).

Core Idea
Sounds and pains are generated, not directly produced, by human power.

This lesson examines the fourth branch of the discussion on tawleed: sounds and pains resulting from human actions. The author argues that such effects cannot occur directly but only through generation, because they depend on physical interactions like pressure and collision. The response challenges this by reinterpreting these as conditions rather than causes, and the debate extends to compositions (taʾlīfāt). Understanding this branch clarifies the broader theory of causality in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the flow from claim to response to further discussion.
Pay attention to the terms 'tawleed', 'mubāshara', and 'shurūṭ'.
2
Identify the main claim and its basis
The passage argues that sounds and pains from human actions can only occur by generation. Why? Because they require physical interactions like pressures and collisions. If they were direct, they wouldn't need these.
The key premise: sounds are inconceivable without physical contact.
3
Analyze the response and the cause vs. condition distinction
The response denies that these physical interactions are causes; instead they are conditions (shurūṭ) for the direct act of power. Understand this shift in terminology.
This is a typical Ashʿarī move to preserve direct divine power while acknowledging apparent causal links.
4
Examine the debate on compositions
Abū Hāshim adds all compositions as generated because they depend on contiguity. Abū ʿAlī distinguishes: compositions within the locus of power (e.g., pressing your own fingers) are not generated; those outside are generated.
Identify the boundary: acts that occur entirely in a locus external to the agent's power require generation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الرابع) من تلك الفروع الأصوات والآلام الحاصلة بفعل الآدميين لا تحصل إلا بالتوليد، إذ لا يعقل وجود صوت إلا باعتمادات لبعض الأجرام على بعض واصطكاك بينها وكذا الحال في الألم الحاصل من الآدمي فلو كانت هذه الأمور واقعة بطريق المباشرة لما توقفت على هذه الأسباب. والجواب : لا نسلم أنها أسباب، بل جاز أن تكون شروطاً لوقوعها من القدرة مباشرة، وزاد أبو هاشم التأليفات على الإطلاق لتوقفها على المجاورة فتكون متولدة منها، وجوابه ما عرفت آنفاً، ومنعه أبو علي في التأليف القائم بجسمين هما أو أحدهما محل القدرة كمن ضم أصبعه إلى أصبعه أو ضم أصبعه إلى جسم آخر وقال : هذا التأليف يقع بغير توليد بخلاف التأليف القائم بمحلين غير محل القدرة، كجسمين مباينين لمحلها فإنه لا يقع بغير التوليد لأن الفعل الصادر عن العباد في محل خارج بتمامه عن محل قدرتهم لا يكون مباشراً بالاتفاق بين القائلين بالتوليد.
The fourth of these branches: sounds and pains resulting from the actions of human beings do not occur except by generation (tawleed), for it is inconceivable that a sound exists except by pressures (iʿtimādāt) of some bodies upon others and collision between them, and likewise the case of pain resulting from a human. For if these things occurred by direct causation (mubāshara), they would not depend on these causes. And the answer: we do not grant that they are causes; rather, they may be conditions (shurūṭ) for their occurrence from power directly. And Abū Hāshim added compositions (taʾlīfāt) absolutely, because they depend on contiguity (mujāwara), so they would be generated from them. And his answer is what you already know. And Abū ʿAlī denied it concerning composition subsisting in two bodies, both or one of them being a locus of power, like one who presses his finger against his finger or presses his finger against another body, and said: this composition occurs without generation, unlike composition subsisting in two loci that are not the locus of power, like two bodies separate from its locus, for it does not occur without generation, because the act issuing from servants in a locus entirely external to the locus of their power is not direct by agreement among those who affirm generation.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth branch dealing with sounds and pains that result from human actions. The claim is that these can only occur through generation (tawleed), not direct causation, because sounds require physical pressures and collisions, and pains require similar physical processes. If they were direct, they would not need such causes. The response denies that these are causes; instead, they may be conditions for the occurrence of the effect directly from divine power. The text then discusses Abū Hāshim's addition of compositions (taʾlīfāt) as generated, and Abū ʿAlī's objection that some compositions, like pressing one's own fingers, occur without generation because the act is within the locus of power. The argument follows a pattern of claim, objection, and differentiation between compositions within and outside the locus of power.

المباشرة (mubāshara)
الاعتماد (iʿtimād)
التأليف (taʾlīf)
المجاورة (mujāwara)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: One might think that sounds and pains are directly produced by human power.
Correction: This passage argues they are generated through physical conditions, not directly.

A) Causes
B) Conditions
C) Neither
D) Both

Key Takeaway
The fourth branch teaches that sounds and pains are generated (tawleed) because they depend on physical conditions, not directly caused by human power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
You often think you directly cause outcomes through your actions. But this lesson says you are only an agent within a limited locus, and most effects are generated. How will you recalibrate your reliance on God tomorrow, starting with the sounds you make and the pains you may cause?

The Two Types of Generating Causes
Enumeration of two types of generating causes with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of generating causes according to those who affirm generation (tawleed), along with concrete examples.

Core Idea
Causes generate either only at inception or continuously, depending on their nature.

In the study of causality, scholars who accept the notion of generation (tawleed) classify causes based on when their effect occurs. Some causes produce their effect only at the very moment they arise, while others continue to generate as long as they persist. This lesson presents that classification with examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage that defines the two types of generating causes. Notice the structure: first the general division, then examples for each.
The passage uses 'إلى ما توليده في ابتداء حدوثه' and 'إلى ما توليده حال حدوثه ودوامه'.
2
Identify the first type and its example
The first type generates only at inception. The example is contiguity (mujāwara) generating composition and weakness (wahy) generating pain. Understand why they are considered inception-only.
These causes produce their effect instantly, but the effect does not require the cause to persist.
3
Identify the second type and its example
The second type generates both at inception and continuance. The example is the necessary pressure (iʿtimād) of a lower body generating downward motion. Note the condition 'if no preventer prevents it'.
The effect continues as long as the cause persists, unless obstructed.
4
Reflect on the classification principle
Consider why the classification matters for understanding causality. It shows that not all causes behave the same way in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بالتوليد قسموا السبب المولد إلى ما توليده في ابتداء حدوثه دون حال دوامه وإلى ما توليده حال حدوثه ودوامه إذا لم يمنعه مانع . فالأول : كالمجاورة المولدة للتأليف والوهي ( أي تفرق الأجزاء المبنية بنية الصحة ) المولد للألم فإنهما يولدانهما حال الحدوث لا حال البقاء . والثاني : كالاعتماد اللازم للسفلي فإنه عند انتفاء الموانع يولد الحركة الهابطة حال حدوثه ودوامه، قال الآمدي : ذهبوا إلى ذلك ولم يعلموا أن كلا من المجاورة والوهي في ابتدائه كهو في دوامه فإذا لم يكن هناك مانع من التوليد لزم من عدم توليدهما في الدوام عدم توليدهما في الحدوث، ومن توليدها في الحدوث توليدهما في البقاء ولو أخذوا خصوص الابتداء أو ما لازمه في التوليد لزمهم ذلك في جميع الأسباب المولدة ولم يقولوا به.
Those who affirm generation (tawleed) divided the generating cause into: that whose generation is at the beginning of its occurrence without the state of its continuance, and that whose generation is at the state of its occurrence and its continuance if no preventer prevents it. The first: like contiguity (mujāwara) that generates composition (taʾlīf) and weakness (wahy)—i.e., the separation of parts built with the intention of soundness—which generates pain; for they generate them at the moment of occurrence, not at the moment of continuance. The second: like the necessary pressure (iʿtimād lāzim) of the lower [body]; for when preventers are absent, it generates downward motion at the moment of its occurrence and its continuance. Al-Āmidī said: They held that view and did not realize that each of contiguity and weakness at its beginning is the same as at its continuance. So if there is no preventer of generation, it follows from the non-generation of the two in continuance their non-generation in inception, and from their generation in inception their generation in continuance. And if they took particular inception or its necessary concomitant for generation, that would apply to all generating causes, but they do not hold that.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage classifies generating causes (al-sabab al-muwallid) into two types: those that generate only at the moment of their inception and not during their continuance, and those that generate both at inception and continuance as long as no preventer exists. Examples: contiguity and weakness generate composition and pain only at inception, while the necessary pressure of a lower body generates downward motion continuously. Al-Āmidi critiques this distinction, arguing that contiguity and weakness are the same at inception and continuance; if no preventer exists, consistency demands that generation either always occurs or never, but the proponents do not apply their principle universally. The passage thus presents a classification followed by a logical objection.

Key Terms
السبب المولد (al-sabab al-muwallid)
التوليد (tawleed)
المجاورة (mujāwara)
الوهي (wahy)
الاعتماد (iʿtimād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all generating causes produce their effect continuously as long as they exist.
Correction: This lesson shows that some causes, like contiguity and weakness, generate only at inception, not continuously.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a generating cause that produces its effect only at the moment of inception?
A) Necessary pressure of a lower body
B) Contiguity (mujāwara)
C) Both A and B
D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
Generating causes are classified into those that generate only at inception and those that generate continuously, depending on their nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain from an injury, do you realize that the cause (weakness) only produces pain at the moment of injury, not continuously? How does this truth about physical causes point you to depend on Allah for every moment of relief and healing?
?
Think of a habit or sin you keep falling into. According to the second type of cause, as long as the inner desire (like pressure) remains and no preventer (fear of Allah, self-control) is present, it continuously generates action. What preventers have you failed to install, and will you set them today?
?
The passage distinguishes between causes that act only once and those that act continuously. In your own life, are you treating some good deeds (like a single prayer or charity) as if they continuously generate reward without ongoing effort? How does this lesson correct your understanding of consistent worship?

Al-Āmidī's Critique of the Classification
Al-Āmidī's logical objection to the classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Āmidī's logical critique of the classification, arguing that the distinction is inconsistent.

Core Idea
If no preventer exists, causes should generate consistently whether at inception or continuance.

The classification of generating causes into inception-only and continuous may seem intuitive, but al-Āmidī points out a logical flaw. He argues that the examples given (contiguity and weakness) are the same at both states, so if generation fails in continuance, it should also fail at inception. This lesson examines his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique passage
Read al-Āmidī's objection. Note that he begins by summarizing the proponents' view, then presents his logical argument.
The passage uses conditional reasoning: 'لزم من عدم توليدهما في الدوام عدم توليدهما في الحدوث'.
2
Understand the key premise
Al-Āmidī asserts that contiguity and weakness are the same at inception and continuance — their nature does not change over time.
If the cause is identical, then any difference in generation must be due to an external factor, not the cause itself.
3
Follow the logical implication
If no preventer exists, then either generation occurs at both times or at neither. The proponents' claim that generation happens only at inception is therefore inconsistent.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the classification leads to a contradiction.
4
Consider the broader application
Al-Āmidī adds that if the proponents took 'specific inception' as the relevant property, it would apply to all generating causes, but they do not hold that.
This shows that their classification is not based on a consistent principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بالتوليد قسموا السبب المولد إلى ما توليده في ابتداء حدوثه دون حال دوامه وإلى ما توليده حال حدوثه ودوامه إذا لم يمنعه مانع . فالأول : كالمجاورة المولدة للتأليف والوهي ( أي تفرق الأجزاء المبنية بنية الصحة ) المولد للألم فإنهما يولدانهما حال الحدوث لا حال البقاء . والثاني : كالاعتماد اللازم للسفلي فإنه عند انتفاء الموانع يولد الحركة الهابطة حال حدوثه ودوامه، قال الآمدي : ذهبوا إلى ذلك ولم يعلموا أن كلا من المجاورة والوهي في ابتدائه كهو في دوامه فإذا لم يكن هناك مانع من التوليد لزم من عدم توليدهما في الدوام عدم توليدهما في الحدوث، ومن توليدها في الحدوث توليدهما في البقاء ولو أخذوا خصوص الابتداء أو ما لازمه في التوليد لزمهم ذلك في جميع الأسباب المولدة ولم يقولوا به.
Those who affirm generation (tawleed) divided the generating cause into: that whose generation is at the beginning of its occurrence without the state of its continuance, and that whose generation is at the state of its occurrence and its continuance if no preventer prevents it. The first: like contiguity (mujāwara) that generates composition (taʾlīf) and weakness (wahy)—i.e., the separation of parts built with the intention of soundness—which generates pain; for they generate them at the moment of occurrence, not at the moment of continuance. So if there is no preventer of generation, it follows from the non-generation of the two in continuance their non-generation in inception, and from their generation in inception their generation in continuance. And if they took particular inception or its necessary concomitant for generation, that would apply to all generating causes, but they do not hold that.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage classifies generating causes (al-sabab al-muwallid) into two types: those that generate only at the moment of their inception and not during their continuance, and those that generate both at inception and continuance as long as no preventer exists. Examples: contiguity and weakness generate composition and pain only at inception, while the necessary pressure of a lower body generates downward motion continuously. ?

The Dispute Over Tastes and Colors
Introduction of a dispute over generated tastes and colors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central question raised in the seventh branch: whether tastes and colors resulting from human actions are generated from those actions or not, and the significance of the conditional term 'sharṭan'.

Core Idea
Are tastes and colors created by human action? That is the core dispute.

This brief passage from the Seventh Branch presents a foundational question about causality and human action. Theologians debated whether sensory qualities like tastes and colors that result from human actions (e.g., striking) are generated by those actions or not. The passage ends with the ambiguous word 'sharṭan,' suggesting a nuanced condition that requires further exploration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how it introduces a dispute without elaborating on the positions.
The passage is very brief and may be incomplete.
2
Identify the central question
The passage asks: 'are they generated from his action or not?' Understand that 'tastes and colors' refer to sensory qualities that arise from physical actions like striking.
The term 'mutawallida' (generated) is a key technical term in kalām.
3
Reflect on the word 'sharṭan'
The passage ends with 'sharṭan' (conditionally). Consider what condition might be implied—perhaps the generation is conditional on some factor, or the dispute itself is conditional.
This ambiguity signals that the full position requires more context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(السابع : قد اختلفوا في الطعوم والألوان التي تحصل بالضرب وغيره في أفعال العبد هل هي متولدة من فعله أولا شرطاً
The seventh: They differed concerning tastes and colors that occur by striking and other things in the actions of the servant: are they generated from his action or not? Conditionally?
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage introduces the seventh branch as a dispute over whether tastes and colors resulting from human actions (like striking) are generated from the action or not. The text is very brief and ends abruptly with 'conditionally' (sharṭan), which may indicate a condition or the beginning of a gloss. The exact position is not elaborated within this window.

Key Terms
الطعوم (ṭuʿūm)
الألوان (alwān)
التوليد (tawleed)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the dispute is about all tastes and colors in the world, regardless of human action.
Correction: The passage specifies tastes and colors 'that occur by striking and other things in the actions of the servant,' i.e., those directly resulting from human actions, not natural or divine phenomena.

Checkpoint

A) Whether tastes and colors are generated from human actions
B) Whether sins are generated
C) Whether sounds are generated
D) Whether death is generated

Key Takeaway
The seventh branch explores whether tastes and colors from human actions are generated from those actions, with the word 'sharṭan' hinting at a conditional nuance.

The Non-Uniformity of Generation
Gloss criticizing al-Āmidī's argument on generation consistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the generation of contiguity and weakness differs from that of pressure, and how this critique defends the Muʿtazilī position against al-Āmidī's objection.

Core Idea
Generation is not uniform; the nature of the cause determines its pattern.

This gloss addresses al-Āmidī's challenge that if generation occurs at the moment something comes into existence, it should also occur during its continuation. The commentator points out that this is not true for all types of generation; specifically, the generation of contiguity (mujāwara) and weakness (wahy) are different because their effects are singular and do not multiply. By working through this short but dense passage, you will see how this distinction protects the Muʿtazilī view from al-Āmidī's objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Āmidī's claim
Begin by anchoring the passage: it is a response to al-Āmidī's argument that generation should be the same at inception and at continuance. The phrase 'from their generation in inception their generation in continuance' is the claim under scrutiny.
Al-Āmidī's argument is not fully quoted; the gloss assumes familiarity.
2
Observe the counterexample of pressure
The gloss first examines generation from pressure (iʿtimād): at inception it generates motion from the start, but at continuance it generates motion from the midpoint. This shows that generation can differ in effect over time.
This is the key analogy to contrast with contiguity and weakness.
3
Contrast with contiguity and weakness
Unlike pressure, contiguity and weakness cannot have such differentiation. For contiguity, composition (taʾlīf) is a single entity and does not multiply. For weakness, pain is one even if it persists. Hence, their generation pattern is uniform.
This is the core of the rebuttal.
4
Reach the conclusion
The gloss concludes that al-Āmidī's objection does not refute the Muʿtazila because the cases are different. Even if one grants the premise, weakness can occur during recovery without pain, further showing disanalogy.
The final line 'for the apparent difference' summarizes the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومن توليدهما في الحدوث توليدهما في البقاء إلخ) فيه نظر إذ توليد الاعتماد حال الحدوث للحركة من المبدأ وحال البقاء للحركة من الوسط ولا يمكن مثله في المجاورة، فإن التأليف لا يتعدد ولا في الوهي فلان الألم واحد وإن دام ولو سلم فالوهي متحقق عند البرء مع انتفاء الألم فلا يرد على المعتزلة ما أورده الآمدي لظهور الفرق .
His statement: 'And from their generation in inception their generation in continuance, etc.'—there is consideration in it, for the generation of pressure (iʿtimād) at the moment of inception is for motion from the beginning, and at the moment of continuance for motion from the midpoint, and the like is not possible in contiguity (mujāwara); for composition (taʾlīf) is not multiple, nor in weakness (wahy), for the pain is one even if it persists. And even if granted, weakness is realized at recovery while pain is absent, so what al-Āmidī raised against the Muʿtazila does not apply due to the apparent difference.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This gloss responds to a statement (likely al-Āmidī's) arguing that if generation occurs at inception, it should also occur at continuance. The gloss points out that generation from pressure differs: at inception it produces motion from the start, at continuance from the midpoint, but such differentiation is not possible for contiguity and weakness because composition and pain are singular and do not multiply. Furthermore, weakness can occur without pain during recovery. Thus, al-Āmidī's objection does not refute the Muʿtazilī position, as there is a clear distinction between the cases.

Key Terms
التوليد (tawleed)
الاعتماد (iʿtimād)
المجاورة (mujāwara)
الوهي (wahy)
التأليف (taʾlīf)
الألم (alam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that generation must be uniform across all causal relationships; al-Āmidī assumed this.
Correction: The gloss shows that generation is not uniform; the nature of the cause (e.g., pressure vs. contiguity) determines whether its effects differ over time.

Checkpoint
According to this gloss, why does al-Āmidī's objection not apply to contiguity and weakness?
A) Because they are not real causes
B) Because composition and pain are not multiple
C) Because generation is always continuous
D) Because pressure is not a generating cause

Key Takeaway
The generation of contiguity and weakness is not parallel to that of pressure because their effects are singular, so al-Āmidī's objection that generation must be uniform fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen that even in physical causality, effects are not uniform—they differ by context and nature. What does this teach you about the uniqueness and wisdom of Allah's creation? How does this humble your own assumptions about how things should work?

The commentator points out that pain and weakness can be separate, even though we often tie them together. Is there a 'pain' in your life (a hardship or sin) that you have assumed must always bring 'weakness'? Could it be that Allah has separated the two for a greater purpose? Reflect and commit to reconsidering one such link today.
?
Al-Āmidī's error was to demand uniformity where the Creator placed diversity. Where in your religious practice have you demanded that your experiences match someone else's or a standard you set, instead of accepting the unique way Allah guides you? What will you do to let go of that demand and trust His specific design for your life?

The Generality of Generating Causes: Not All Causes Are Equal
Gloss defending non-uniformity of generating causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that not all generating causes require the same conditions; some are conditional while others are not, based on intuition or reasoning.

Core Idea
Causes are not all alike; some need conditions, others do not.

In this gloss, the author responds to an objection that the principle of generation should apply uniformly to all causes. He clarifies that intuition (badāha) or reasoning (naẓar) may determine different rules for different causes. This lesson unpacks that argument and its significance for understanding causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Notice the objection and the response.
The passage is short but dense; pay attention to the examples.
2
Identify the objection being addressed
The gloss begins by referencing an objection: that the rule of generation should apply to all generating causes uniformly. Find where this is stated.
The objection is implied in the phrase 'لزمهم ذلك في جميع الأسباب المولدة'.
3
Understand the distinction between causes
The response distinguishes two types of causes: those that require a condition (like weakness) and those that do not (like pressure). Examine how intuition or reasoning leads to this distinction.
Weakness (الوهي) requires the absence of a preventer; pressure (الاعتماد) generates unconditionally.
4
Reflect on the role of intuition and reasoning
Consider why the author appeals to badāha and naẓar as the basis for determining causality rules. This shows that causation is not arbitrary but grounded in rational assessment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لزمهم ذلك في جميع الأسباب المولدة قد عرفت مما ذكرنا آنفاً أن البداهة أو النظر قد يوجب الاشتراط في البعض كما في الوهي ويحيل في البعض كما في الاعتماد فلا يلزمهم ما ذكر في جميع الأسباب المولدة .

So what he mentioned does not apply to all generating causes.

Commentary
This gloss responds to the objection that the principle of generation should apply uniformly to all generating causes. It argues that intuition (badāha) or reasoning (naẓar) may require different treatments: some causes, like weakness, need a condition (e.g., absence of preventers) for generation, while others, like pressure, generate unconditionally. Therefore, the objection does not hold universally.

The Two Types of Generating Causes
Classification of generating causes into two types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between causes that generate effects only at inception and those that generate both at inception and continuance.

Core Idea
Some causes only spark, others sustain.

The proponents of tawlid (generation) needed to explain how causes produce their effects. They observed that not all causes work the same way. Some, like placing a heavy object on something, cause an effect only at the moment of contact, while others, like gravity, continuously generate motion. This lesson introduces that classification and its examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses a very compact style with parentheses.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage presents two types of generating causes. Find the definition for each type and note how they differ.
The first type is described as generating only at the beginning of its occurrence, the second type generates both at occurrence and continuance.
3
Analyze the examples
For each type, the author gives examples: adjacency and separation for the first, pressure for the second. Think about why these examples fit the definition.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this distinction matters for the theory of causality. How does it affect the understanding of how effects are produced?
This distinction helps explain why some effects are instantaneous and others are sustained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بالتوليد (قسموا ) السبب ( المولد إلى ما توليده في ابتداء حدوثه دون حال دوامه وإلى ما توليده حال حدوثه ودوامه ) إذا لم يمنعه مانع . ( فالأول : كالمجاورة المولدة للتأليف والوهي ( أي تفرق الأجزاء المبنية بنية الصحة ( المولد للألم ) فإنهما . يولدانهما حال الحدوث لا حال البقاء . ( والثاني : كالاعتماد اللازم للسفلي ) فإنه عند انتفاء الموانع يولد الحركة الهابطة حال حدوثه ودوامه، قال الآمدي : ذهبوا إلى ذلك ولم يعلموا أن كلا من المجاورة والوهي في ابتدائه كهو في دوامه فإذا لم يكن هناك مانع من التوليد لزم من عدم توليدهما في الدوام عدم توليدهما في الحدوث، ومن توليدها في الحدوث توليدهما في البقاء ولو أخذوا خصوص الابتداء أو ما لازمه في التوليد لزمهم ذلك في جميع الأسباب المولدة ولم يقولوا به.
قوله : ( ومن توليدهما في الحدوث توليدهما في البقاء إلخ) فيه نظر إذ توليد الاعتماد حال الحدوث للحركة من المبدأ وحال البقاء للحركة من الوسط ولا يمكن مثله في المجاورة، فإن التأليف لا يتعدد ولا في الوهي فلان الألم واحد وإن دام ولو سلم فالوهي متحقق عند البرء مع انتفاء الألم فلا يرد على المعتزلة ما أورده الآمدي لظهور الفرق .
Those who affirm generation (tawlid) divided the generating cause into that whose generation is at the beginning of its occurrence without the state of its continuance, and that whose generation is at the state of its occurrence and its continuance, if no impediment prevents it. The first: like adjacency (mujawara) generating composition (ta'lif) and separation (wahy) – i.e., the dispersion of parts built with a healthy structure – generating pain; they generate them at the moment of occurrence, not at the state of continuance. The second: like the necessary pressure (i'timad) for downward movement; for when impediments are absent, it generates downward motion at the moment of its occurrence and its continuance. Al-Amidi said: They went to that, and they did not know that each of adjacency and separation at its inception is like it in its continuance; so if there is no impediment to generation, then from non-generation of them in continuance follows non-generation of them in inception, and from generation of them in inception follows generation of them in continuance. And if they took particular inception or its necessary concomitant in generation, that would be necessitated for them in all generating causes, and they did not say it. His statement: 'And from their generation in inception, their generation in continuance, etc.' – there is consideration in it, for the generation of pressure (i'timad) at the moment of inception is for motion from the beginning, and at the moment of continuance for motion from the middle, and the like is not possible in adjacency, for composition is not multiple, nor in separation, for pain is one even if it continues. And if conceded, separation is realized upon healing with the absence of pain, so what al-Amidi brought does not refute the Mu'tazila due to the apparent difference.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage defines the two kinds of generating causes in the theory of tawlid, presents al-Amidi's objection, and then answers it. The author first states that the proponents of generation divided causes into those that generate only at the onset of the cause (like adjacency and separation generating composition and pain) and those that generate both at onset and during continuance (like pressure generating motion). Al-Amidi argues that this distinction is unfounded because if there is no impediment, generation should be continuous for both types. The responder points out that pressure generates motion differently at initiation and continuation, which is not analogous to adjacency or separation, and that the pain from separation is continuous but disappears upon healing, showing a difference. The argument is logical and uses examples.

Key Terms
توليد
سبب مولد
مجاورة
وهي
اعتماد
تأليف
ألم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All generating causes produce their effects continuously as long as they exist.
Correction: Some causes, like adjacency and separation, only generate their effects at the initial moment of their occurrence, not during continuance.

Checkpoint
In the classification, which of the following is an example of a cause that generates its effect only at the moment of inception?
A) Pressure (i'timad)
B) Adjacency (mujawara) and separation (wahy)
C) Both pressure and adjacency
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Generating causes are of two kinds: those that cause an effect only at the moment of their existence, and those that cause an effect both at inception and as long as they persist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a habit or action in your life that had a powerful effect at first but then seemed to lose its impact. Which type of generating cause does it resemble? What does this lesson suggest about the need for a different kind of cause—one that produces effect continuously—to sustain lasting change? Consider what that continuous cause might be in your spiritual life.
?
The lesson distinguishes between causes that generate only at the moment and those that generate continuously. Reflect on your relationship with Allah: Are there acts of worship or obedience that you perform that are like the first type (one-time) and others that are like the second (sustained)? How does this understanding challenge you to examine the consistency of your devotion?

Al-Amidi's Objection and Response
Al-Amidi's objection and the refutation from the nature of the effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's logical objection to the classification of generating causes and the counter-argument that distinguishes the cases.

Core Idea
Al-Amidi argued the distinction is arbitrary, but the response shows a key asymmetry.

Al-Amidi, a prominent Ash'ari theologian, challenged the Mu'tazilite classification of generating causes. He reasoned that if there is no impediment, the cause should generate its effect continuously, so the distinction between inception and continuance collapses. This lesson presents his objection and the careful response that points out how the examples differ in kind.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Amidi's objection
Read the Arabic passage from 'قال الآمدي' to the end of his argument. Then read the translation. Focus on his logical inference.
Al-Amidi uses a conditional argument: if no impediment, then generation at inception implies generation at continuance, and vice versa.
2
Understand the objection's structure
Identify the premise (that adjacency and separation are the same at inception and continuance) and the conclusion (that the distinction is invalid).
3
Read the response
Read the second part of the passage (starting from 'قوله'). The response points out the difference in the examples.
Notice how pressure generates motion differently at inception and continuance, while composition and pain are singular events.
4
Evaluate the response's strength
Consider whether the response successfully refutes al-Amidi's objection. Think about the examples of healing and the non-multiplicity of effects.
The response also adds that separation disappears upon healing, showing it is not continuously generated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Death from a Wound: Can It Be Generated?
Dispute on whether death from wound is generated, with arguments and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute over whether death from a wound is generated from its pains and the key arguments from consensus and scripture.

Core Idea
Generation of death from a wound tests causal theory and divine exclusivity.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the sixth issue in the discussion of tawlid: whether death resulting from a wound is considered generated (mutawallid) from the pains. This dispute reveals a tension between the principle of tawlid (ordering of cause and effect) and the Islamic consensus that only God is the creator of death and life. We will see how each side attempts to ground its position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic and English translations to get the overall argument. Notice the two opposing positions.
The passage includes marginal commentary, so read carefully.
2
Identify the two positions
Some deny that death from a wound is generated (mutawallid); some affirm it. Note the reasoning given for each.
3
Understand the denier's inconsistency
The denier rejects generation, yet the text argues that if death follows pains, it should be generated according to the principle of tawlid. This exposes a logical inconsistency.
4
Understand the affirmer's problem
The affirmer accepts generation, but this contradicts the consensus that only God is the independent giver of life and death, and contradicts Quranic verses.
The commentary explains that life is mentioned to prevent Mu'tazilites from claiming power over life as well.
5
Reflect on the implications
This dispute shows how theological commitments (divine exclusivity) can conflict with causal theories (tawlid).
Think about how each position tries to uphold one principle at the expense of another.

(The Sixth: They disagreed concerning death generated from the wound – i.e., occurring after it – whether it is generated from the pains generated from the wound. A group denied it, and others affirmed it. The denier of it opposes his own principle in generation, because the ordering of death after pains necessitates its generation from them as in other generated things. The affirmer of it opposes the consensus, for the community unanimously agreed that the One independent in causing death and life is God Almighty, and it opposes the Book, for its texts indicate it. Commentary
This passage discusses the sixth issue concerning death generated from a wound. The author states the disagreement: some deny that death is generated from the pains caused by the wound, others affirm it. The denier is accused of inconsistency because within the theory of tawlid, if death follows pains, it should be generated from them. The affirmer is accused of opposing consensus and scripture, as the Quran attributes life and death solely to God. The marginal notes elaborate on the implications: if death is within human power, then life might also be claimed to be within human power, contradicting the divine attribute of being the sole giver of life.

The Dispute on Tastes and Colors from Actions
Dispute on whether tastes and colors from actions are generated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions on whether tastes and colors generated by human actions are considered 'generated' from the action, and the key argument and counter-argument.

Core Idea
Whether tastes and colors come from actions depends on the similarity of bodies and the role of accidental conditions.

This lesson explores a classic debate in kalam about causality: when a human strikes a substance like molasses, does the resulting taste and color count as 'generated' from the human's act? Two schools of thought offer opposing answers, and their arguments reveal deeper principles about the nature of bodies and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes a marginal note at the end.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents two groups: those who affirm generation of tastes and colors from actions, and those who deny it. Find the reasons each gives.
The affirmers say it occurs by the action and according to it.
3
Analyze the deniers' argument
The deniers argue that if generation were true, then beating would produce taste/color in every body because bodies are similar. Understand the logic behind this.
They assume homogeneity of bodies.
4
Examine the response
The responder counters by noting that differences in accidents can be conditions for generation, so the property does not occur in bodies lacking those conditions.
This introduces the concept of 'shart' (condition).
5
Reflect on the marginal note
The marginal note points out an inconsistency: the deniers accept pain as generated from striking, yet striking a stone does not produce pain. Consider why this weakens their argument.
This shows the deniers' own theory is not universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(السابع : قد اختلفوا في الطعوم والألوان التي تحصل بالضرب) وغيره في أفعال العبد هل هي متولدة من فعله أولا وذلك ) كلون الدبس وطعمه الحاصلين بضربه بالمسواط ( عند طبخه ( فأثبته قوم) :وقالوا مثل هذا الطعم واللون متولد من فعله ( لحصوله بفعله ) وعلى حسبه، ( ومنعه آخرون ) وقالوا : لا يقع شيء من الألوان والطعوم من العباد لا مباشرة بقدرتهم وهو ظاهر ولا متولداً من أفعالهم ( وإلا لحصل ذلك الطعم أو اللون ( بالضرب ) أو نحوه من أفعال العبد في كل جسم، لأن الأجسام (متماثلة لتركبها من الجواهر الأفراد المتجانسة ) فيقال لهم : ( بعد تسليم تماثل الجواهر ( لم لا يستند ) حدوث الطعم واللون المتولد من فعل العبد في بعض الأجسام دون بعض ) إلى اختلاف أعراض فيها شرط لحدوث ذلك اللون والطعم فيه ) ، فلا يحدث شيء منهما في جسم آخر لم يوجد فيه شرط وإن تعلق به ذلك الفعل.

(The Seventh): They disagreed concerning the tastes and colors that occur by beating and other actions of the servant – whether they are generated from his action or not. That is like the color of molasses and its taste occurring by beating it with a mallet when cooking it. A group affirmed it, saying such taste and color are generated from his action because they occur by his action and according to it. Others denied it and said: No color or taste occurs from servants, neither directly by their power – that is apparent – nor generated from their actions; otherwise, that taste or color would occur by beating or similar actions of the servant in every body, because bodies are similar due to their composition from homogeneous individual atoms. So it is said to them: After granting the similarity of atoms, why does the occurrence of taste and color generated from the servant's action in some bodies but not others not be attributed to the difference of accidents in them that are a condition for the occurrence of that color and taste? So nothing of them occurs in another body in which the condition does not exist, even if that action is connected to it. His statement: 'Otherwise that would occur, etc.' is refuted by the non-occurrence of pain by striking a stone, while pain is generated from striking according to them, so reflect.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This is the seventh issue concerning tastes and colors. The author presents two positions: some affirm that such properties are generated from actions because they occur by the action and are proportionate to it. Others deny and give a reason: if generation were true, then every body beaten should acquire that taste/color, since bodies are similar. The responder counters by noting that differences in accidents (accidental properties) in bodies can be conditions for generation, so the property does not occur in bodies lacking those conditions. The marginal note criticizes the deniers' argument by pointing out that their own theory of pain generation from striking is not universal (e.g., striking a stone does not produce pain), yet they still affirm generation of pain. So their objection is inconsistent.

The Generation of Pain: Pressure or Separation?
Dispute on whether pain from pressure is generated from pressure or from separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate between the majority Mu'tazila and Abu Hashim on whether pain is generated directly from pressure or from the separation (wahy) caused by pressure, and the reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Pain's true cause is separation, not pressure—but both views claim empirical support.

The eighth issue in the discussion of generation (tawālīd) concerns a specific phenomenon: pain resulting from pressure, such as a blow or cut. Theologians differed on the immediate cause. Is pain directly generated from the pressing action, or is it generated from the dispersion (tafarruq al-ittiṣāl) that the pressure causes? This passage presents two positions and the arguments for each, revealing deeper principles about causation in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views on pain generation
Read the passage and note that the majority holds pain is generated from pressure (iʿtimād) while Abu Hashim holds it is generated from separation (wahy).
This disagreement is within the Mu'tazila.
2
Examine Abu Hashim's argument
Abu Hashim argues that pain intensity correlates with the degree of separation, not the intensity of pressure. He uses the example of a delicate member vs. a compact member.
This empirical observation is key to his position.
3
Analyze the majority's response
The majority counters that variation in separation from a single pressure is analogous to variation in pain from a single separation, so the argument does not prove pain is from separation.
They claim symmetry undermines Abu Hashim's reasoning.
4
Reflect on the marginal note
The marginal note clarifies the proper form of the response and indicates further debate, showing the scholastic method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثامن): قد اختلفوا في الألم الحاصل من الاعتماد على الغير بضرب أو قطع فقيل : إنه يتولد من (الاعتماد) وهو مذهب جمهور المعتزلة ، ( وقال أبو هاشم في المعتمد من قوليه : إنه يتولد من الوهي ) وكأنه أخذه من قول الحكماء : سبب الألم تفرق الاتصال ) والوهي ) يتولد ( من الاعتماد ) وذلك ( لأن الألم بقدر الوهي قلة وكثرة لا بقدر الاعتماد ولذلك يؤلم الاعتماد الواحد العضو الرقيق الرخو أضعاف ما لم يؤلم ) العضو ( القوي المكتنز وما هو إلا لاختلاف ما يوجب ) ذلك الاعتماد ( فيهما من الوهي)، فإن التفرق الحاصل منه في الرخو أكثر وأقوى من الحاصل في المكتنز فلا يكون الألم متولداً من الاعتماد بل من الوهي لأن خاصة التوليد اختلاف المتولدات بحسب اختلاف أسبابها ) والجواب أن اختلاف الوهي المتفاوت ( في القلة والكثرة والقوة والضعف ( من الاعتماد الواحد كاختلاف الألم المتفاوت من الوهي الواحد ) والصحيح كما في الأبكار من الاعتماد الواحد أي أن كلا منهما اختلاف في أمر متولد من شيء واحد بلا اختلاف فيه ( فلم يستند هو) أي اختلاف الألم على تقدير تولده من الاعتماد ) إلى اختلاف القابل كما استند ) إليه ( اختلاف الوهي ( على ما اعترفتم به والحاصل أنكم جوزتم استناد الوهي في الاستدلال
قوله : ( والجواب أن اختلاف الوهي الأظهر في العبارة أن يقال : والجواب أن اختلاف الألم كاختلاف الوهي، لأن النزاع وقع في تولد الألم واعلم أن هذا الجواب إنما هو عن بالوجه المذكور، وأما لو استدل أبو هاشم على دعواه بترتب الألم على الوهي المترتب على الاعتماد فإن ترتب فعل على آخر هو معنى التوليد احتيج إلى الجواب الذي سيذكره بقوله : وأيضاً فيبطله إلخ، لكنه أيضا غير تام كما ستطلع عليه .
(The Eighth): They disagreed concerning the pain that occurs from pressure (i'timad) on another by beating or cutting. It was said that it is generated from the pressure, which is the doctrine of the majority of the Mu'tazila. And Abu Hashim said in the well-known of his two opinions: It is generated from separation (wahy), and it is as if he took it from the statement of the philosophers: the cause of pain is the separation of connection. And separation is generated from pressure. That is because pain is according to separation in quantity and intensity, not according to pressure. Therefore, one and the same pressure harms the delicate, soft member many times over what it does not harm the strong, compact member. That is only due to the difference in what that pressure causes in them of separation. For the dispersion occurring from it in the delicate is more and stronger than that occurring in the compact. Thus, pain is not generated from pressure but from separation, because the property of generation is the variation of generated things according to the variation of their causes. The answer: that the variation of separation – varying in quantity, intensity, and strength – from one and the same pressure is like the variation of pain – varying from one and the same separation. And the correct thing, as in the first positions (al-abkar), is from one and the same pressure – i.e., each of them is a variation in something generated from one thing without variation in it. So it (i.e., variation of pain, on the assumption that it is generated from pressure) is not attributed to the variation of the recipient as the variation of separation is attributed to it, as you have admitted. The conclusion: you have allowed the attribution of separation in the argument. His statement: 'The answer that the variation of separation...' The clearer expression would be to say: The answer is that the variation of pain is like the variation of separation, because the dispute is concerning the generation of pain. Know that this answer is only regarding the mentioned aspect. But if Abu Hashim argued for his claim by the ordering of pain upon separation which is ordered upon pressure, then ordering an action upon another is the meaning of generation, then the answer that he will mention by his statement 'And also it invalidates it, etc.' would be needed, but it is also incomplete as you will see.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This is the eighth issue on the generation of pain. The two views: majority say pain is generated from pressure itself; Abu Hashim says it is generated from separation caused by pressure. He argues by empirical observation: pain correlates with degree of separation, not pressure intensity. The response from the majority is that variation in separation from the same pressure is analogous to variation in pain from the same separation, so the argument fails. The marginal note further discusses the proper form of the response and indicates that a further argument by Abu Hashim using ordering (tartib) would require another answer.

تفرق الاتصال

Misconception: Pain is always proportional to the intensity of pressure.
Correction: Abū Hāshim shows that pain depends on the degree of separation, not directly on pressure.

Key Takeaway
The debate on pain generation illustrates how kalām uses empirical observation and analogical reasoning to determine causal relationships.
