Refuting the Objection on Actions and Miracles
Sanusi presents and refutes the objection that attributing all actions to God makes miracles unreliable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing all actions to God does not undermine miracles or prophethood, and how the concept of 'customary continuity' (adah) preserves the evidential value of miracles.

Core Idea
God's actions are beyond good and evil; miracles are safeguarded by custom, not by impossibility.

A key objection against Ash'ari causality is that if God creates all actions, including evil ones, then miracles could be performed by liars, breaking the link between miracles and prophethood. Sanusi presents this objection and then refutes it by arguing that ugliness does not apply to God and that miracles are preserved by customary continuity. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer introduced by 'قلنا'.
2
Identify the objection's two consequences
The objection claims that if God does ugly acts, then (1) a liar could receive a miracle, and (2) lying about God becomes possible, destroying trust in His speech. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the two-part answer
The answer has two parts: (a) ugliness does not apply to God, so the premise is false; (b) even if a liar's miracle were rationally possible, it is known not to occur by custom (adah). Reflect on how each part refutes the objection.
The second part relies on the concept of customary continuity discussed earlier in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : من تلك الأمور نسبة فعل العبد إلى الله تعالى فلانه يلزمه كونه فاعلاً للقبائح فجاز حينئذ إظهار المعجزة على يد الكاذب، إذ غايته أنه فعل قبيح وقد جوزتم صدوره عنه تعالى فلا يبقى للمعجزة دلالة على صدق النبي وجاز أيضاً الكذب عليه سبحانه فيرتفع الوثوق عن كلامه في وعده ووعيده وفيه إبطال الشرائع بالكلية، قلنا : قد أجبنا عنه بما مر من أنه لا قبيح بالنسبة إليه تعالى بل الأفعال كلها يحسن صدورها عنه، ومن أن إظهار المعجزة على يد الكاذب وإن كان ممكناً صدوره عنه عقلاً إلا أنه معلوم انتفاؤه عادة كسائر العاديات إلى آخر ما مر في البحث عن كيفية دلالة المعجزة.
The second: From those matters is attributing the action of the servant to God Almighty, because it necessitates Him being the doer of ugly acts. Then it would be permissible to manifest a miracle at the hand of a liar, since its ultimate is that it is an ugly act, and you have permitted it to occur from Him, so the miracle no longer indicates the truthfulness of the prophet. And it would also be permissible to lie about Him, so trust in His speech regarding His promise and threat is lost, and this entails nullifying the laws entirely. We said: We have already answered this with what has passed: that there is no ugliness with respect to Him, rather all actions are good coming from Him; and that manifesting a miracle at the hand of a liar, although possible in reason, is known to not occur customarily, like other customary matters, as passed in the discussion on the modality of the miracle's indication.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection: if God creates all actions, including human evil acts, then God does ugly acts, which would break the link between miracles and truthfulness (since a liar could also get a miracle), and would lead to distrust in God's promises. The answer reiterates that ugliness is not applicable to God; all His actions are good. As for miracles appearing via liars, it is rationally possible but known not to happen due to customary continuity (adah), as previously discussed.

Key Terms
القبيح
المعجزة
العادة
الإبطال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates all actions, including evil, then miracles could be performed by false prophets, so we cannot trust any miracle as a sign of truth.
Correction: The author explains that ugliness does not apply to God, so the objection is invalid. Additionally, miracles via liars are known not to occur by custom, so miracles still reliably indicate truthfulness.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the argument that attributing all actions to God would allow miracles to appear via liars?
A) By denying that miracles can occur via liars
B) By stating that it is rationally impossible
C) By saying that it is known not to occur customarily
D) By claiming that God never does ugly acts

Key Takeaway
Attributing all actions to God does not undermine miracles because God's actions are beyond moral categories, and miracles are safeguarded by customary continuity, not rational impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you hide behind the thought that 'God created this action, so it's not really me'? The lesson says God's actions are beyond blame, but yours are not. How will you take full ownership of your sins today and repent, rather than shifting responsibility?
?
If you truly believed that God's promises are as certain as the sun rising (because of customary continuity), would you worry less about your future? Will you consciously affirm that trust in Him is not based on rational proof but on His consistent habit, and commit to acting on that certainty in one specific decision this week?
?
This lesson shows that even a rationally possible event (a liar's miracle) is rejected by custom. Are there 'rational possibilities' you entertain to doubt God's truth? What would it look like for you to submit your intellect to God's customary way and stop seeking loopholes?
